RATING: 5
Played a 5 player game for the first time, everyone but me had at least played once, which took 4+ hours. I think it's a soundly designed game, but I think it might kill Ameritrashy FFG games for me. 

I played with blue which seems really underpowered, and because of a fluky event card deck, placing corruption was very, very difficult for 90% of the game. On the other hand, red seems to be very overpowered, and eventually won by leveling up. I didn't feel like I was in control, or in competition, for almost the entire game. My choices were limited, and didn't provide me much benefit that I could see. I don't find the theme or artwork very appealing either. 

The components are nice, but I can't really think of much during my four hours of playing that was anything but frustrating and unpleasant. I had initially said I would try it again some day, but after sleeping on it that is doubtful. We'll see.

Shame it's basically a 4p only game.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
At the moment this is my favourite boardgame, for many reasons:
1) very strong asimmetry: each faction plays very differently from the others (different cards, same type of units, but each one with different characteristics and upgrades, very different strategies, some are more geared toward certain winning conditions than the other, but a good player may win in more than one way).
2) depending on the setup, each player can go for one of the 2 winning conditions (or even both). The only exception is nurgle, which looks like he can win much more easily by points than by dial advancement. This results in games which can be much different from each other.
3) the theme is very strong and well integrated: each god has cards and upgrades that fit very well into their role in the warhammer universe.
4) even though some gods (I'm looking at you, khorne) look particularly strong in the beginning, each game I played was very tense, and at least 2 players were almost tied for the win.
5) There is dice combat, but it isn't too much random (there's usually a 50% chance of success), and khorne is the only one who actively looks for a lot of fights.

There are also a couple of negative aspect:
1) a decent knowledge of the behaviour of the gods is required to play in the best way. I've found games with some new players to be quite balanced, but if there is a single experienced player he will win.
2) in one game, i was playing khorne and had a lot of very bad luck witch dice in the first half of the game, resulting in no dial advancement  (and no points) in the first three rounds, effectively cutting me out of the game.  Don't be too scared, since I was playing with an experienced player and he told me something like that never happened to him.

RATING: 9
Chaos in the Old World is another hybrid game combining area control mechanics with asymmetrical race balances and dice-rolling.
The game really shines in its fair amount of time, it can be played and a game mechanism which nearly guarantees tense games and photo finishes every play.
The one and really con is that its pretty much restricted to 4 players, because all different gods are pretty much needed to keep the game balance and leaving one god out, will favor the one or the other remaining.
The game wont get boring after a lot of plays, which is another proof for well developed games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
It is an area control/influence game based in the Warhammer universe. You control one of four faction and compete to fulfill different objectives. Every turn you corrupt the landscape, dominating its inhabitants, and battle with the depraved followers of rival gods. Each god has a unique deck of gifts and abilities, and can upgrade their followers into deadly foes. Summon forth living manifestations of Chaos, debased and hidden cultists, and the horrifying greater daemons - beings capable of destroying near everything in their path. You have multiple ways of winning the. The downside however is that it plays well only with four players, otherwise it becomes unbalanced. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I finally got a chance to play this game, and I really liked it. I can see why you have to have 4 to play, but this game left me wanting more. I'm excited to try it again with my regular group.

What I really liked about this was that each player is seeking victory in a different way, and those different paths look to be very balanced. The game itself is an area control game mostly, with randomized setup and events throughout the game. On top of the mostly Euro underpinnings, FFG has layered a ton of theme, and some ameritrash elements. 

I'd put this game into the same category as Dungeon Petz as a Euro/Ameri hybrid, with elements of both.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Suffers from a few optimal plays in early turn figure placement and optimal upgrade cards. 

The dice honestly seem like they really don't fit this game at all. Everything else is so exacting and then you throw a very random wrench in the gears. The buckets of dice system is awfully random. Combined with Khorne's dial being one kill per territory you can easily get one dial advancement counter one turn and five the next with the exact same setup. Or you can get six kills in one territory on two dice (yes, I've had this happen several times). It's just wacky. 

In a theoretical second edition I'd like combat to be a different system. I'd love this game to be diceless but it would require a separate combat deck or something with major reworkings. 

update: I think I've come to terms with the dice but I'm still not fond of the exploding on 6s thing as it can get kind of swingy. I like the threat of more kills than the number of dice because it keeps the planning from getting too finely tuned, but I would like it better  if it were limited in some way. 

probably final update, as I haven't played in years: Chaos seemed brilliant when I was first starting to get into boardgaming as a serious hobby. It showed me the light. I still love the theme but my opinion has dropped from great to merely good and my enthusiasm for play has diminished accordingly. If someone wanted to play Chaos I would do so, though I would rather not play Khorne since so many of his decisions are rote. As it stands I would not request a play except perhaps in a rare fit of nostalgia. I have too many games with the same amount of interaction but with less rough edges and balance issues. Plus, I have a friend with a small stack of COIN games which fill an identical niche.

We did have an long discussion about the end game conditions, and decided that Check the End Game sub-phase is the only time where we evaluate whether the game is over or not. We did discuss this point extensively, however, since the "anytime" word in the rulebook threw us off. 

If it comes down to a 4 player game to play, I think it'll come to this one (due to rarity of 4 player game nights). If we have our usual 5+ players, I think we'll be looking at Small World (for a shorter game when we want to play multiple games in one day) or Shogun (for those marathon, one game days).

RATING: 9
Rated after three repeated plays. I usually never feel that I have to replay a game when I finish one session, but this was amazing. First play you get to know the roles, second play you tried a strategy that failed miserably, but it would have worked if only... So you have to play just one more time, but this time you try another God with different strategy, and someone else is doing something you never would have though of with your former God... It just goes on. 

I like the mix of euro worker-placement and ameritrash dice-slaughter. Its both and neither.

Maybe it will grow old in the end, but this game also feels very balanced. If I do not discover a radical strategy that ruins this balance, I look forward to many sessions of CitOW.

Note: I knew nothing of the Warhammer universe before buying the game, two of the people in my group did. I did not feel it was an issue for me, the game stand very well on its own.

RATING: 8
I do not like the art of Chaos. It turned me off from the game for awhile because it's just a little bit too over the top with its dark fantasy theme. But Chaos really does have some fascinating gameplay. I like the asymmetrical factions a lot. They have such different goals and abilities to struggle for victory. I'm okay with the design philosophy of if they're all equally broken, then they're equally balanced. 

But some races do seem strictly stronger and weaker (red faction is just annoying how much they will stomp rookie players by sheer force of exploding sixes and silliness). Chaos is a rough, darkly themed game that I can't really recommend very widely. But I still find the dynamic of the unique factions to be very neat.

RATING: 7
3-5(4,5)

It's solid. Nice area control mechanics and very interesting player powers and scoring objectives. Issues for me are that, yes, Khorne can be a bit intimidating, but his play is sort of boringly straight-forward not much thinking going on. Additionally, the other players can often just sort of try to claim their nooks (with their relevant tokens and such) and the only interaction is avoiding Khorne. I'm certain, though, that after some plays it will become obvious that you'll need to start crippling other (non-Khorne) players. As it was, I've played Khorne and thought it was frustratingly boring because it was pretty obvious what I needed to do to win and subsequently players made the "obvious" moves of going out of their way to give me nothing to attack. Also, it is important to know all the decks and I can see how some would prefer having a community action deck to pull from (for balance reasons).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Fantasy Flight has always produced strongly thematic games.  With Chaos in the Old World they have at last managed to do this without encumbering the game with a mass of cards, chits and special rules. You'll play to a finish in a reasonable time too.

At heart Chaos... is simply an area control game with a touch of Risk-esque combat.  Its cleverness lies in the distinctive powers and goals given to each of the Chaos Gods contesting the game.  Each plays very differently and yet the game feels well balanced.  Add to this a different selection of Old World (event) cards every game and there's a good degree of variability.

While the artwork is suitably thematic, the borders between areas can be difficult to see and some areas are just too small.  Production quality is up to the usual FFG standards.  The rulebook is the usual FFG verbose.  Do yourself a favour and download Universal Head's excellent summary, in which he fits into two pages what sprawls over 25 in the FFG booklet. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Area control game with very asymmetric powers, great mechanisms, lots of potential in this game (if you don't mind the theme), but...

First play, Khorne seemed massively overpowered, or at least, it wasn't obvious until too late how to counter him. Didn't make for a terribly satisfactory first play. On future plays, is it all about how well the other three players together prevent Khorne's double-dial-advancement win? Not sure if that's the most fun game dynamic. Will a new player (and there's basically always at least one, usually more, new players in the groups I play in) usually hand the game to Khorne?

When I finished my first play I wasn't sure how keen I was to play again, but actually now I feel I want to play again to see how viable stopping Khorne is when you go into it with your eyes open. Provisional rating a 7, but this could definitely go up or down.

RATING: 9
I understand why people love or hate the game. Although I am not attracted by his other designs, I believe that Eric Lang blew the competition out of the water with this. Not for every palate. This game has a well defined storyline and players have to struggle to push the events in their direction to be the winner. You know that Khorne is going to win if left unchecked. You know Nurgle is going to dominate. Slaanesh will probably try to root in a noble rich region and tick those precious dials. Tzeetch will outmanouver you. Small decisions will tip the scale and your ability to react to them will decide whether you're going to survive or not. Not for fans of "best move" or optimisation games.

[Updated March 2012] 
I have begun painting the miniatures for this beloved game.  May also consider replacing the corruption chits with wooden tokens.

[Updated June 2012]
I have ordered a complete replacement for the influence tokens in the form of laser-etched 3/4" disks with the chaos star etched into them.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
First of all, this wasn't a game on my wishlist at all, i just found a very good deal and gave it a try. Do I regret it? Defenitely not. I played this game a few times now and can really say that it is pretty intuitive and a nice hybrid between the classical Area of Control mixed with Euro Elements. What stands out for me is the high player interaction across the board and the different playstyles depending on the chaos god you get. Another thing which is phenomenal is the variety of winning the game. Every single path seems to work (sure some fractions have an easier times winning on certain paths).
Even if Chaos in the old world is a great game, i have to mention some slight cons about this game. 
First of all, you really need the full playercount (4) to play this game correctly. The design pretty much makes this a must, because of upcoming balancing issues if not played with this count. Second: The picking of upgrade cards seems a bit scripted to me, because most of the time there is really 1 card strictly better than the other. Third and this is not really a con, but from my point of view the most important factor of making this game really good. You need a Gaming Group, which really has the ability to keep track of the game and adapt accoridingly, while not overly adapting and completely crushing the leader. Chaos is so dependand on an ingame self-balancing mechanism, that the game can be screwed if someone heaviley messes up. Overall this is a great game and can be often played because of its lower playtime (1-2 hours). Another thing to mention, the game doesnt come down to only skill in the end. The final result is more dependand on the gaming group (the meta) itself, then just pure right decisions in the end. So if you are on the high end of competitive play, this might be a con for you. Overall i can see this Game easily rated between 5 and 10 depending on the gaming group. Luckily i have a very passionated Group where most decisions are rationally based so for me its between the 8-10 range.
I'd highly recommend it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.75
The love-child of Euro and AT, and managed to inherit all the best traits of both!  Solid area control/action point mechanics make this a strategic game, but when combined with asymmetric (and balanced!) powers, direct conflict, and even a little bit of role-play (with the dials), it makes for a very fresh experience that I enjoy even when I lose.  Also stands as a fantastic example of theme-to-mechanics continuity, even if the theme is a bit objectionable at best.

My only complaints are, as I mentioned, the theme (I still squirm when someone plays Rain of Pus...yuck), and the 4-player only dilemma.  Other than that, pure genius!

Edit - Now with Horned Rat!  Takes the delicate balance of finely tuned 4-player world destruction and impressively transforms that into finely tuned 5-player world destruction.  What more could you want!?

RATING: 6.5
The details around the design of this game are fascinating to me.  Love the asymmetric nature of the player 'gods'.  Like how the game play reflects the different gods and their methods / objectives.  Play is straight forward enough to introduce to newer players and the quality of the game pieces is top notch. My nick on the design of the game is that I am not sure that each god has multiple paths to victory.  It is entirely likely that each player has to follow a prescribed course tactic - and if one doesn't it might not cost just them the game, but might throw off another player as well - setting up a third for the win simply because the 2nd couldn't survive.  This makes the game have a real sense of 'controlled chaos'  and given the game's name - perhaps that is the desired state.  Good game, but it might frustrate some.

RATING: 8
A well crafted area control game with a great theme.  El Grande with tentacles.  I like how each player will have the opportunity to explore unique strategies based on the role they assume (Much like Here I Stand in this respect).

A tight and clear rule set combined with short playing times means that this game can hit the table often and a appeal to a broad range of gamers.

Chaos In the Old World doens't seem to present anything particularly new, but it still feels like a fresh approach to some tried and true game mechanics.

Ultimately this is a fun game, but I'd rather play El Grande.  Not because Chaos In The Old World is bad, but becuase El Grande is just so good.

now, I dislike AT quite a lot, but still I was surprised how much worse this game seemed than starcraft or even the underveloped conan. 

seeing how FFG can't decide how powerful the factions are, and the swing is HUGE one way or the other, it's not easy to believe the game was meant to be at all balanced.

RATING: 9
The best asymmetrical area control game where players have to balance/cancel/neuter each other in hopes of trying to steal a victory at the end.  The theme is perfect for this game as this game requires you to be nasty to one another.  This game doesn't have King making per say.  I would consider it as players need to police each other if they are to have a chance of winning.  The play time is perfect as the game is deep enough to command your attention the entire time without overstaying it's welcome.  Downtime is not an issue as well, which is remarkable considering the complexity of the game.  While the game feels complex, it feels entirely simple at the same time.  The only draw back to this game is that it should be played at max player count.  If the game was more flexible with the player count it would be 10 for sure.

RATING: 8.3
Area influence the ameritrash way! Very good medium level game. There is a lot of combat but the game is mostly about manoeuvering your demons around the board trying to use your powers to their maximum ability to gain dominance in the different regions. This will give you points and point can win you the game. Where this game really shines is the other way you win the game. Each player has a threat dial which if it is advanced enough times also wins you the game. The gods you can play are totally different which makes them fun to play out against each other. Downside is that most gods seem designed for a single strategy and cannot be played any other way. Game is quick, fluent and a lot of fun. (if you find destroying the world funny that is) Components are generally good but the miniatures are very breakable (again).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
For me, Chaos hits all the right spots.  It's asymmetric, has multiple paths to victory, is highly thematic and it lasts just the right amount of time and has just the right amount of conflict and diplomacy.  Fighting is useful, but it's not the path to victory (unless you're Khorne), so it avoids issues of player elimination or beating up a weak player - in fact, you want to avoid beating up a weak player since you'd rather weaken someone else's position.  Finally, if one of your guys dies, it's not good, but it doesn't really cause you to fall behind since you have a limited pool of minions to summon from.

I'm taking a point off because of the poor map design though.  Combined with the piles of corruption tokens that you have to place, it makes for a very crowded map!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Pro's:

1) Entirely biased but as a big fan of warhammer fantasy and my main army being chaos deamons it isn't exactly surprising that  I think the theme is amazing.
2) Multiple winning conditions and asymmetry of the powers provides extra variety, then add the upgrades + the way the spells interact with each god and you have consistently shifting chaos that pretty much drips theme.
3) I love the simplicity of the miniatures as well as the aesthetics of the spell cards, corruption tokens and the board itself.
4) While the game is competitive there is also a ticking clock that forces the players not to damage each other's plans entirely as otherwise the board wins, I seriously love these types of mechanics in games.

Con's:

1) Pretty much exclusively balanced for 4 players, each god balances out another god and so it is pretty much unplayable otherwise. Seriously, do not play the game at anything less than 4.
2) Game can be punishing if one of the players does not play their role correctly in balancing their counterpart god. This means you can have an issue where one players is simply able to dominate the game easily (Seriously, this is more punishing than the COIN games as at least those are long enough that screwing up an individual turn wont necessarily lose you the game). Chaos on the other hand could easily make it so you have no chance of winning the game at all.

RATING: 9
I’m still undecided about Chaos in the Old World: thematically it’s very rich, which is one of the very few things I really enjoy in a game; as far as playing it goes though it turns out as an area-control game that doesn’t play any different from your typical dry and unthematic euro.

And this is what makes me mistrust this otherwise excellent game: it actually feels like an euro in disguise, which means you can surely mess with the other players if you wish, like any good game requires, but in the end it’s the point-grinding only what really counts, thus the less you cross your opponents’ path the most points you collect and the closer to winning you get.

In other words, Chaos in the Old World still doesn’t feel chaotic enough to claim its pretentious title. [i](January 2013)[/i]

[b]Update[b] - Since only one of my gaming buddies owns Chaos in the Old World, I haven’t had many chances at playing it recently but I’ve had time aplenty to brood over it and change my mind: it’s more of a hybrid game than an euro, since so many things can get out of control as soon as you run out of luck with dice rolls or card draws; moreover, the different focus and abilities of the four gods make for plenty of randomness and different paths to victory.

Ultimately I feel Chaos fully belongs to the hybrid games like Cyclades, among which it shines: hence during my Fall 2015 rating revise not only raised I my rating from 7,5 to 9 but Chaos also convinced me it’s chaotic enough to fully deserve its title.

RATING: 9
This is an outstanding game. The mechanics that are combined actually combine to create a game engine which works cohesively. Each decision is meaningful; each moment is tense. Most importantly, each side plays and feels differently; playing with the god's personality actually makes sense strategically. The theme is the opposite of pasted on with this game as it is, in fact, integrated into the play of the game. I know nothing about the Warhammer universe but didn't need to to rate this game a ten.

Update: I still keep the game at a nine, but I don't get it to the table as much I'd like. It seems to me that what I like about the game - the asymmetry of the different factions is actually improved upon so strikingly by Cthulhu Wars. Khorne seems to stand as playing one way while the other gods are variations on dropping corruption. Their card play options differ but their overall goals remain pretty symmetrical. It's still an outstanding game and one of my all time favorites, but Cthulhu Wars does asymmetry so much more boldly that it has made me aware of some missed opportunities in CitOW.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Four asymmetrical powers, area control, strong themeing and unabashed face-mashing-direct-player-adversarial game play.  I love it!!  DO NOT play this with people that get pissed with direct confrontation and in-your-face gameplay.  

The more I play this game, the more I love it.  The powers are very different, and each game plays out differently than any of the other ones.  As we get more experience the game continues to change and players start doing unexpected things.  I love, love, LOVE this game!  It truly is a unique mix of mechanics and works so very well on many levels.  A classic and a game I can see hitting the table many, many more times.

I've been trying to figure out the allure of this game.  I believe that one of the things that I like about it is that when playing with the same group of players the game really evolves.  It's difficult to go into the game with a specific winning tactics in mind (well, the Khorne player tends to have the same strategy, but the tactical approach must be different) and actually play those tactics and win.  The randomness of the old world cards and the initial token placement has a lot to do with the actions of the players.  Since the game actively encourages the players to foil each others plans whenever possible, it really fosters a competitiveness that many games are unable to.  Tack on an interesting and over-the-top theme with solid mechanics and you have a classic game!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Provisional rating, after 1 and an aborted play ... thought I'd like this, didn't :(

I think my expectations were way off what this game is, and that's probably the root of the problem. I was expecting a game with battling gods, powers being launched and mayhem. And got instead a euro engine with a huge gob of chaos (appropriate for the name, maybe, but I didn't like it) from player actions.

Weirdly I found it a pretty dull game that annoyed me while playing! That's odd because usually a dull game I just want it over and have no interest or 'investment' in the game - which makes me think maybe I'm being too harsh on it and should give it another try (hence the 4 rather than the 3 rating).

It's a euro at its heart, it could easily have been an economic game with cubes etc instead of plastic minis and a 'war' theme.

Combat is pretty dull, no 'tactics' other than roll 6's ... at least it's fairly quick.

What annoyed me, each turn you just optimise your actions, there's not much in the way of borders, everything is free-form. Events bring chaos, you might get stuff to help you might not. Player actions bring chaos, plenty of options for random screwage ... and kingmaking, you're out of the running but can seriously influence who wins.

Just got irritated playing, which isn't why I play games - couldn't find the fun at all :(

*** Update, played again, bit better experience this time, although I'm not convinced it works with 3, powers seem setup to balance each other out, with one missing one power has a bit of pressure lifted. So now it's at a stage where I'd play it again, but only 4 player ... and see how I feel. Rating could rise (but not that high I think) or fall ... can't make up my mind.

*** Updated, played again, I think it's better with 4 - I still don't feel like a chaos god playing it though, theme feels a bit pasted on, it could so easily have been something else. No desire to own a copy, would play (4 player) again though, rating is now a 5.

**** Thanks to trading I now own a copy and I've played it more and my opinion has changed, a bit, game is solid, but players need to know what they are doing and how each power wins, otherwise it can be a bit of a blow out. Still think the theme is a bit pasted on though.

**** Finally played it again, and liking it more - shame I can't get it to the table more often :(

RATING: 6.5
Only one play so far of my friends' copy of this. I know that it may take a couple of times through before I really get the interplay of the systems here, but I can't help but feel that this game is too bloated and old fashioned to stack up to it's new, flashier cousins. It sometimes feels like Blood Rage, but really is it's own beast, and that beast is a strange, chess-like interplay of systems that demands that you play each race a certain way. I'm sure that some may love that feel to the game, but I did not. It's too restrictive, and I never got the pay off of really "learning" how to make my race tick within a single game. It is deep, and I'm sure given enough time I would have that Ah-ha! moment, so for now I will concede that More Data is Required, but I also have a sinking suspicion that this one is not for me.

RATING: 5
Okay as an AT game. The "balance" seems to be the big debate. Difficult to assess based on my one game. It's effectively an area-control/area-influence game with asymmetrical powers and goals. Some card driven mechanics and some simple dice rolling for battles.

Nothing overly innovative. The 'chrome' will be attractive to ATers. If they had been cubes of 'caballeros', then this game would have been trashed by euro-haters. Likewise, it's not better than some of the new editions of Risk - and the gameplay is not that different. But the Risk name is old school and the poster child for BGG bashing, so CitOW is now the flavour of the month that is all the rage and excitement.

As for strategy, doesn't the race that you play pretty well dictate how you play the game?

Update Jan 2010 to Nov 2012. Dropped from 6.5 to 5. Gave it a second chance. It's just meh.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2.2
Sadly my rating for this has dropped dramatically since my initial play. This game IMHO is massively overrated and typical of the recent over-euorised chromed up games. To many moving parts, cards, variables and conditions to keep track of. A fairly long playing time as well. The game in essence boils down to a clay pigeon crap shoot with virtually no planning or long term strategy to follow due to the random chaotic nature of the cards, combat system and whims of the other players. This is exacerbated by a 50/50 bingo roll combat system. Effectively Korn is trying to hit the other races on 50/50 rolls and the others are trying to avoid this. When you strip it down, that in summary, is all it actually is. There is also almost no point in other races using military pieces as Korn can simply allocate hits on the cultists first and ignore the other units. Pure luck which is made even more extreme by the bizarre cards. The swings are ridiculously huge as if Korn hits a lot, it gets dial advancements AND denies the other races their advancements. So it is actually a double swing. I spent 3 hours lining up cultists and just hoping Korn did not place units/cards there and/or hit. Please anyone tell me where the fun is in that. It really amazes me how a game like this can be rated in the top50 despite all this. If you want to play a quick combat game there are far better around.

RATING: 8.5
A fantastic game of majorities.  There is a constant see-saw throughout between the various aspects of the game.  The variety of winning conditions make for a fascinating game where a play can strategise early about how they want to win the game and then enjoy the tense ride as they tactically deal with the ebb and flow of action over the course of the game.

Turns go quickly and there is little downtime.  The various Player Powers all play differently and the game will always run out a little differently each time.

Altogether - Chaos in the Old World is a very good game.

RATING: 7
I thought that this was a fun game.   I tend to like games where each player has unique goals and abilities, and this fit that mold nicely.   

It's certainly a game with some replayability because of this, so I look forward to playing it again.

It is dripping with theme, and the little character pieces are quite atmospheric.


Note on 2nd play (and rating dropped to 7 from 8):

I found the game a little frustrating in that it does appear that all players must be extremely aware of what other players are doing relatively early in the game to stop someone from taking a future lead that can become unsurmountable.

If purple or red get 2 rounds where they win dial-majority, the game is effectively over, and even a clever point victory can't win it for you.

I found myself multiple times reminding other players of the condition/strategies of opponents (and getting a little frustrated at their lack of urgency) to attempt to get them to help me stop such a lead, with mixed results.

In any case, I probably came over as being manipulative or pushy when I was just trying to prevent a lead that could not be stopped. I really dislike games where you are forced to campaign against other players. What was pretty obvious to me was not obvious to other players (and in a way was NOT obvious because it dealt with an early advantage turning into a bigger advantage later on), which just made me look like a pushy dick.

In this way, it does appear to be a very fragile game. One mistake by one player at the right time can enable an unstoppable future victory for another player. It is also incredibly easy to king make (either intentionally or not) for almost any player as the game end approaches.

RATING: 7
Tough game to rate. Quite random. Graphic design and presentation (except for the minis) is awful - map is hard to parse, fonts are unreadable as always, rules are the usual FFG incomprehensible sinkhole. The cool things are the 4 different factions with their own dials, event decks, and so on. The bad news is that the factions are pretty railroaded; Khorne particularly just plops down Bloodletters and tries to rack up dial advances, but the other factions aren't much better and are all more or less trying to do the same thing (score points for ruination while spending as little effort as possible to get the dial advances they need). It's all fine, it works pretty well actually, and there is nothing else quite like it (well, maybe GMT's COIN games, but those have a world of their own problems). But for me, there is nothing in there that stands and calls out to be played. I find Asgard's Chosen much more compelling (it's not issue-free either, but it's much more dynamic and thoughtful).

RATING: 9
Theme, mechanics and gameplay are definitely fun. If you like fantasy or Role-playing, looking at the world through eyes of evil is a nice change of theme from dozens of games where you play for the good guys. The game however is tricky in a way that some sides are much easier to master/play than others, which leads to very uneven balance. It could take multiple plays (and loses) to learn strengths and tactics for sides other than Khorne, which is very straightforward and therefore pretty easy. 
My advise would playing without Khorne if playing with less than max number of players, and for other player to keep Khorn in check when playing with max players. The game also have a high tendency for "kingmaker" issue. 
If sides balance could be tweaked a bit, the game would be even more enjoyable.

RATING: 7
People who play with me know that I don’t care for theme at all, so I don’t really mind the Warhammer theme (which I know next to nothing about), though I do think it’s cool that it’s an area control game which doesn’t have a medieval theme or something like that. 

This is a game I had wanted to play for a looong time and finally got it to the table in April – twice! This meant a lot as in my second game I could still remember what the different factions could do. I think one of the most interesting things about the game is just that: the varied and very different factions. However, while the asymmetry is interesting, I’m afraid that the strategies for each faction are more or less dictated from the beginning; Khorne should probably not go for a VP victory… So I fear that there isn’t enough room to play your own game and I would like some more flexibility. That being said, the factions do play out very differently which was a big plus! Tzeentch is definitely my favorite god/race with Slaanesh a relatively close second. As I have only played the game twice (Khorne and Tzeentch), I would definitely like to play it at least three more times to try the two other races and to try Tzeentch again. And it sure isn’t a lot of games I’m going to play 5+ times, so that looks promising.

Furthermore, the 3 different victory conditions (and the possibility that everybody loses, though that’s unlikely to happen) was a great twist! You cannot just focus on ruining the land and getting most VPs if somebody else is close to having moved their little wheel all the way to the finish. In both of my games the games did end when somebody passed 50 points, but in one of the games the game wasn’t far from ending because of the “wheel” victory condition. 

One other problem I had with the game was the potential king making problem. The winner might not be the player who played the best, but rather the player who got picked on the least… The game isn’t your typical euro area control game. This definitely adds a twist of ameritrash though it’s still mainly a euro. I’m a euro guy by heart but I did like the ability to crush your enemies; it’s just a pity that the combat system doesn’t really work. In my second game, the Khorne player almost exclusively rolled 1’s and 2’s and didn’t stand a chance just because of sheer bad luck. I was thinking of buying the game before I got to play it. Now that I’ve played it a couple of times I don’t think I need to own it, and while I wouldn’t actively seek it out, I would be willing to play it a few more times to explore what the different factions have to offer.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
[b]Includes the Horned Rat expansion.[/b]

Rated 7 after one play. I enjoyed this more than I expected. I've recently been looking to play various "dudes on a map" games, and this one was recommended by a number of people.

Bottom line is I like it. I enjoy the asymmetrical sides, the card play is interesting, and having various ways to win is intriguing as well. The game rewards familiarity, so bumbling through a first play was to be expected. I don't really care if the theme involves demons and puss and whatnot... the gameplay is pretty good.

Update: I picked this up in a trade, and I'm glad I did. Now with 4 plays under my belt, each with a different faction, I enjoy even more the assymmetry in the game.

Sometimes things haven't gone my way... some terrible rolls as Korne meant I had a tough time trying to catch up... but every play has been enjoyable. I'm surprised at how much the event cards really shake things up and create a new game each time.

Update: Two more plays and this one is a keeper, even though I might be able to sell it for a pretty penny now that the FFG license for Warhammer has expired. This is the best example of asymmetrical powers done right.

RATING: 5
First impression had this one on par with El Grande and far better than Liberte. As with many area majorities games, there is so much smoke you think there must be fire. But the essence is pretty straighforward min/max question; merely the calculation requires a lot of inputs. The card-play certainly adds a richness to the majorities game, generating both tactical variety and player asymmetry. Oddly, I did not feel very disadvantaged by a lack of card-knowledge; they seem quite balanced in their way - a quite lovely feature of the game. The asymmetry of the player goals also creates a nice texture to boardplay and the incentive calculus. While the game does many things right, it still comes down to the fact that I don't have sufficient interest in this type of game to generate the patience necessary to work through board positions and find the best move. This is likely my shortcoming and not the game's, but I'm going to have to start this one off at a very personal, but somewhat uncharitable 5.

RATING: 8
I didn't pay much attention to this game when it was first announced.  No interest in Warhammer.  However, I got tagged to run a game for FFG at GenCon and so had to learn the rules without the game and only a week out to boot.  Once I read them though, I thought this would be a good game.

After running it and explaining it later to my wife, I have to say this is an awesome game.  It is very tight rules-wise and has an interesting valuation concept between the resistance and conquest value.  There are just enough old world tokens to affect all the different aspects of the game while being able to keep them all straight.  The interaction between the 4 Chaos gods is also pretty well balanced.  The set amount of rounds make this a fast-paced game where you have to strike fast and grab what points you can while making sure you advance your threat dial every turn at least once, if not twice.

This game went from a "no interest" to a must have" in a matter of minutes.  I think this is the sleeper hit of the Con for FFG.  If it doesn't get its due, it is only because it is overshadowed by the hype surrounding Middle Earth Quest.

RATING: N/A
 Chaos in the Old World was the first game we ever played on FNGN. Proclaimed as one of "The Most Balanced Games of All time" In part, I'm sure to the due diligence of the magnificent play testers. This board game is larger than life. The stakes are high, and the powers are enormous as these ancient gods do battle in each their own way to rule the Old World, that has no chance of survival no matter the victor. A series of complimenting game play mechanics make this a rewarding experience: combat, magic, special abilities, old world cards, nobles, tasty peasants, dial ticks, and RPG style upgrades unique to each god. Each god plays completely differently, adding to the replay value of an already deep and rich game. Not to mention an expansion that invites a fifth god into the mix, reinvents and re-balances the existing gods, thus changing the game completely...

RATING: 3
I apparently don't see what feller Eurogamers see in this. It did nothing for me. The asymmetry of players feels more annoying than gamey. I don't think the complexity over El Grande adds interesting decisions. In fact between the dice and the cards, you really can't take your decisions too seriously anyway. So a lot of it boils down to a crap shoot. Despite winning my first and only game, I have no desire to play again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Initial rating after my group's first play (4 players). Wow. This was fun. A LOT of fun. The rulebook is clear and accessible and the mechanics just seemed... right. Not one of us had the slightest inkling of what the whole Warhammer Fantasy setting was about (or of ANY Warhammer setting), yet everyone instantly - and gleefully - embraced his or her Chaos god's character and relished the conflict that ensued. Gameplay flowed nicely, despite the fact that we had to consult the rulebook very often (it was a learning game for all of us). In fact, we were all amazed at the amount of time that had passed (over 2 hours with all the rule-checking), because we'd all had such a great time. After the game, a lively discussion of Chaos In The Old World's merits ensued, with players discussing mechanics, possible strategies, the game's artistic merits, etc., etc., etc. Sadly, due to people's schedules, we'd started late and had to break early... or we would have gladly played again.

All in all, Chaos in the Old World enjoyed the most auspicious debut of any game my group has played in the two years or so we've been together. I look forward to seeing how well it stands up to repeated plays.

UPDATE: Everyone still loves the game! Its only drawback is that it plays a maximum of four, and my group usually numbers six.

Will consider this more with each play.

RATING: 9
Being a fan of the Warhammer world, I really enjoy the theme and play of this game.  The four different roles are nice and diverse, lending to a interesting game where everyone is trying to do a little something different while still vying for territorial control.  The Old World deck adds some randomness, but I believe this adds to the flavor and experience of the game.  The two paths to victory (dial advancement, victory points) are great because of how they affect the metagame.  You have to focus on your own gameplan, but you can't ignore what everyone else is doing.  There is great balance.

RATING: 7
Fun gameplay with not-so-useful components. Map is hard to dilineate certain areas, some of the tokens blend into the map art, and the Threat number on the dials is hard to see unless you're right above it looking down. 

Gameplay is very take-that, with players trying to convince opponents who to attack, or where to place creatures. Variable player powers makes for good replayability and varying strategies. 

Some wierd rules, such as the Ruination bonus for players who place a corruption counter in the region [i]on that turn [/i] despite having no real way to track which counters were placed when. Dial bonus just results in players all agreeing to prevent any one player (or trying to) from getting the most. 

I enjoy the limited access to card play in each region and the different "feel" of each faction. Rules were a little heavier than I originally thought. Not everything is immediately clear, so it's definitely more on the Ameritrash side than the Euro side of the coin. Look forward to more plays of this, even though I have no real interest in the theme (Warhammer). 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
(Gerard, 2011)
4 Jugadors
90 minuts (a la pràctica: 100-180 min)
Tremendo. Híbrid euro-trash, de majories, molt molt asimètric, on cada jugador controla un dels Déus/Poders Ruïnosos de l'Univers Warhammer. Cada jugador intentarà devastar, corrompre, desolar el Vell Món abans que ho facin els altres Déus, lluitant contra ells alhora que contra el taulell.
Les diferents maneres d'obtenir la victòria així com la tremenda asimetria entre els Déus, n'augmenten molt la rejugabilitat.
RATIO DE VICTORIES (20 partides, algunes amb Rata, i algunes amb noves cartes del Caos Morrisleb)
- TAULELL: 1 Victòria
- Khorne: 6 (5xSg + 1xMc)
- Nurgle: 1 (Sn)
- Tzeentch: 1 (G)
- Slaanesh: 3 (2xMc + 1xAlex)
- Rata: 1 (G)
- Inacabades: 5
- Errors o invalidades: 2
Última actualització: 8/8/2016
23/02/2015: Des del primer dia que interpretem malament els Nobles (afecten Slaanesh): cada noble dóna +1 PV quan es domina la regió. El noble NO augmenta la Resistència de la regió. Això ha perjudicat MOOOOLT a Slaanesh (li costava més obtenir supremacia de les Regions amb nobles que ell necessitava per girar la roda, i li donaven menys punts). Fins ara, Slaanesh ha estat molt perjudicat.
8/8/2016: Pots invocar una figura des de qualsevol lloc (tauell o reserva) a qualsevol altre lloc on tinguis alguna figura o adjacent. --> Millor no considerar-ho "moviment", sinó que fas desaparèixer una figura d'un lloc (va a la reserva) i la fas aparèixer en una altra banda.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A hybrid game, that has the outside look of an Ameritrash game, but underneath the hood is an example of pure German efficiency. The game itself is one of area majorities where each player has a unique set of playing pieces and special powers. Each piece has a different role, with some responsible for helping to gain majorities, and others primarily about eliminating opponent's pieces. To mix things up, there are ways to win that are only indirectly related to gaining the majorities. These alternate win conditions are usually related to the area majorities, but there is one case (Khorne) where it is about eliminating opponent figures instead. A special event deck adds variety to the game, making this one a fun exercise, and an enjoyable gaming experience.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Players represent one of the four Warhammer old world gods, trying to dominate and ruin the old world.  Quite simple area majority game, where players take turns to place a follower or a chaos card into one of nine regions, spending power points to do so.  Combat then takes place, and regions are assessed for domination, corruption and ruination.  Repeat for eight turns.  Players win by either scoring victory points via corruption and ruination OR by advancing their threat dial to victory - a very neat mechanic that forces each player into asymmetric roles ... i.e. Khorne just kills stuff, but the others are more about corrupting different areas of the board.  I can see why this has it's fans - it's been very well designed and developed, and is intriguing.  I found the 150 minute playing time a bit excessive, and the "Khorne" juggernaut/paranoia tends to dominate the game.  Players have to quickly understand their place in the game, and experienced players will likely slaughter newbies.  The map artist obviously doesn't play the game ... looks nice, but awful to play on.  After two games, it's already beginning to feel a bit slow, dull and repetitive.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
An old favourite and the only game that I ever sold and re-bought. This is an excellent area control game with asymmetrical player powers and a very dynamic board and game state. It's not really akin to risk at all. If you can get beyond the theme (which I like a lot) and some necessary viciousness I can imagine this game will appeal to most groups as it's ultimately pleasant melding of a euro game with a dice driven combat game, and also very thematic, in the way that the daemon's relationship is parasitical and symbiotic. Needless to say: I love this game flaws and all, it offers very high 'replayability', and will get better with successive plays (which I know most groups don't get to due to an intoxication with rampant novelty). 

It's fun to play as the different gods and to get to know how to play against them, but at the same time its fun to commit to one and stick with that. The pleasure in the interplay is very player dependent, there is even some space for alliances and negotiation.

Khorne to me is the most scripted to play and also by far the easiest to win with against inexperienced players. This is a good thing and a bad thing as if you have one new player it makes sense to let them play as Khorne. However someone playing Khorne 'badly' throws off the games delicious balance. On the other hand if you've played the game multiple times Khrone can feel very scripted, and your turn can be very brief as its deadly obvious what you should be doing, that might suit you, I prefer to play as any of the others. 

 Few criticisms of the game, namely that the four player count is much better than three. (I used to have the five player expansion and that was good also though not necessary really as the game doesn't really need additional variety - though it certainly doesn't hurt.) Other criticisms: board can get busy with tokens and minis. I wish the corruption tokens had been cubes so there was less fiddly counting. I wish the map was bigger and easier to read. I wish the minis were better quality and slightly larger. 

This game is better than Blood Rage. Mostly due to it being deeper (also longer) and much more thematic. Blood rage's incredible minis though incredible cause some cognitive dissonance in myself as it never really feels like you are invading anything with them or pillaging anything. Whereas you get a pleasant little-fingerish tickle slowly spreading your corruptive influence/violence across the board in this one, whether through tricksy magic or a simple lust for blood. Also blood rage is too dependent on knowing the cards, which can make the first play quite frustrating. There isn't a great deal of different cards in CITOW, once you've seen a couple of rounds you've seen a good proportion of them. Though in BR defence it plays well at all the player counts, I even liked 2 player, and is overall much more accessible though CITOW all falls into place after a round or two.

If FFG are looking to retheme this one post GW split. I'd go with a game of thrones retheme, with a new map and some different mechanics, or perhaps that would be lame, I don't know.

Made a dumb rule error last time I played, which is when an area is ruined everyone gets the number of points at the top of the card, not just points for first and second. Another common rule error is that you can't advance your dial more than two clicks in a turn and only one person can ever do that in a given turn.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
(acquired in Larry's Great Moving Sale, Part 2).

I haven't seen a game where the theme is this tightly integrated into the gameplay since Dune.  And, since this is fundamentally area control with special powers, it's reminiscent of Dune in that regard also.   The game is mechanically quite simple, but since there are two ways to win (using points, or dial advancements) you really need to keep an eye on the other players and the tension level goes way, way, way up as a result of the dual paths.   Eric Lang is admittedly NOT a Warhammer player, but he absolutely did his homework and hit it out of the park.  The play styles of the 4 Chaos Powers are perfectly captured, and the game design inherently rewards playing to your power's theme and tactics.  Be warned - the game will probably lose significant appeal if you aren't into the Warhammer mythos.  It is sinister and unapologetic.  And that's exactly how Chaos is in the Warhammer world.  Too bad the bubblegum plastic figures are so poorly produced - that's my only real complaint.  Very inspired design, and definitely a long-term keeper.

RATING: 6.5
Wanted to like this more than I did.  Variable player factions and differing objectives are a plus.  It just feels like some of them have a very scripted path to victory (points or dials, but forget about doing it the other way), and some have objectives that are way easier to complete than others.  It's also very cutthroat, since you have to stop the other players from achieving their goals as much as you have to accomplish yours.  
I don't especially like the way that there are only 2 slots for cards at each territory, so you can just get arbitrarily locked out of playing something there if the others beat you to it.
The upgrade system is nice, but kind of frustrating in that there are 5-6 upgrades you can do, some of which get better if you buy them together, but you'll have to work and struggle to get 2 upgrades by the end of the game.
It feels like combat is often going to be a frustrating mess.  You can't really defend, since even if you have a huge army of guys, your opponent can just waltz past them and attack your cultists, killing them off so you can't accomplish anything.  And if you're trying to attack someone in a non-critical area, they could always just re-deploy their guys and run away.
Anyway, it's this weird mix of euro and AT that is partly great and partly awful.

RATING: 8
My first board game that wasn't Monopoly, Checkers or Snakes & Ladders.

Bought this when on road trip through the USA, and played it every night (often multiple times) for a week with three other buddies. 

It's a fun, asymmetric, thematic (if somewhat dark) tactical miniatures game with a very strong Euro bent. 

The asymmetry is what drives this game. Every character plays vastly different. Khorne blindly throws soldiers into fights, and doesn't really care about any outcome other than kills. Nurgle picks a populous region and festers there. Tzeentch tries to interfere with other player's schemes while pursuing his own, and Slaanesh is attracted to nobility and heroes.

The asymmetry is also the greatest issue with this game. We had to house rule Slaanesh plays in second turn order, because of continued complaints that the Slaanesh player was too crippled by playing last. Khorne seems to win a large portion of the games. Indeed, the other players are often forced to work together for the first few turns, or it becomes far too easy for Khorne to win outright. 

These days my friends refuse to play this game, because the final turns suffer from Kingmaker syndrome. Because of the way game victory is resolved, and the inherent strengths of the Khorne and Nurgle characters, a player will often be forced to decide whether Khorne or Nurgle wins. Maybe this is something that can be house ruled out, but it's not something we've explored.

All the same, I still rate this game highly, and recommend it to anyone who thinks the journey is more important than the victor.

RATING: 6.5
Positive Points
---------------
-Asymmetric play and win conditions are really fun
-Nice miniatures and, in general, nice components
-Tons of replayability... would likely take several plays of each god to fully appreciate strategy and tactics and the expansion comes with completely new chaos decks for each god

Neutral Points
---------------
- Resolution of combat, domination and corruption is a bit repetitive

Negative Points
---------------
-Map gets really crowded in some regions with chaos cards, tokens and minis... makes it confusing to sort all out

Overall
-------
Chaos In The Old World is a thematic and thinky dudes on a map game with asymmetric win conditions and play styles. The map can get a little too crowded in some regions, making it difficult to clearly see what is going on and resolution of combat, domination and corruption gets a bit repetitive, but otherwise a really great game. Due to the asymmetric and card driven mechanics, this is a type of game that requires repeated plays to fully appreciate. I would recommend this game above other similar games I've played (e.g. Kemet, Blood Rage and Cyclades) to groups who are looking for a deep game, with lots of replayability (that is, they want to play a game 10-20 times), but it may not be as good as those other games for groups that will play it infrequently (couple times a year).

RATING: 5
This is the decent blend of AT and Euro mechanisms. I say this because on one hand you can try for area majority/VP and can co-exist with certain of your opponents units without resulting in combat. Each god can try for completing objectives for various benefits throughout the game as well as an instant Victory. Direct conflict can also be advantageous through your units/cards. Finding the right mixture of strategy and tactics based on what your opponents are doing is key for victory.  The asymmetrical powers of the four gods are fun to explore.  What makes this an interesting design is that it's a wonderful blend of euro area control with ameritrash combat and ccg-esque card abilities.

Every game has it's flaws. If I was to pick one with this game is that it can be very chaotic. This shouldn't surprise you as the word is part of the games name. No matter how well a person plays, you may not have a chance in winning based on the player induced chaos and potential kingmaking.  While the 4 gods seems to be mostly balanced with one another, in the base game anyway, there are some upgrade cards for each god that are better than others which leads to a bit less choice in the game.

Other things to mention is that there is an action point system in place which I like, dice is used to resolve combat which is ok, and it will not be a 3 hour affair like some other FFG games which is awesome. The visual design and components are heavily themed (and sadly, not very functional at all), though some may be turned off by the theme. 

This used to be one of my favorite games, but it's fallen out of favor due to a very disappointing expansion, some points I haven't mentioned and, various reasons I outline here:

https://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/9780933#9780933

RATING: 8
I traded my El Grande Decennial as a result of this game. Why would I want to play boring old El Grande when this area majority game is soooo much more fun?  The game combines tactical area majority maneuvering with timely card play and variable powers/victory conditions. We're slowly getting better at learning how to keep Khorne in check and drive the game to more of a race between dial tick and VP victory. Downtime is next to nill, and as someone who doesn't like chaos in his games, I totally don't mind it here for some reason. Maybe it's because I have an idea of what the cards are at this point and I don't count on each move but rather throw a bunch at the wall and hope at least some of them stick.

One of the best parts about this game that has yet to fail for us is that it provides a wonderfully strategic experience while causing great table talk and laughter. Not many games can pull off that double whammy of goodness. 

I do have some concerns, however, about the game devolving into kingmaking at the end. I’ve seen more than one game where a player had to choose between Khorne or one of the other players.

RATING: 9
Both in theme and in terms of game mechanics, CITOW gets a 10.  The gods are finely balanced (though some might be trickier to get the hang of than others), and the gameplay is immensely enjoyable.  It's a clear sign that the game is wildly fun when role-playing your Chaos god becomes harder and harder to resist as each turn passes.

My only gripe is that you really need 4 players to play it, and it's very difficult for me to find a full stable of 4 players.  The game *works* with only 3, and remains quite enjoyable, but the balance flies out the window, and some gods instantly become stronger or weaker depending upon which god gets left out.  I also don't like that, for a couple of the gods, at least, it is detrimental to experiment with variant upgrades, especially in the case of my beloved Nurgle (you HAVE to take Provender of Ruin, or you simply can't win).  Because of that, there's little incentive to try out different power combinations.

RATING: 8
One of the better themed games I've played to date, Chaos is one of those few board games where you often unintentionally begin to get in character while playing the game.  This is due in large part to the amazingly balanced asymmetrical gameplay, where each of the Chaos Gods you can play as has a very distinct feel and playstyle to them, yet none of them really seem to be able to dominate the others, since each is able to somehow counter the advantages of another.  The random events going on in the background of the players' struggle for domination over the map ensure that even if you stick with the same God, each game will feel unique.  This game really shines when you get a full game of four players who are able to get past some relatively complex rules and gameplay, to understand their God's advantages and weaknesses and really get into their roles.  Perhaps not for everyone, but highly recommended for those who think being an evil God is even marginally appealing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
A Fantasy Flight game that I don't have to make plans to play two weeks ahead of time? [b]What?[/b] A great, medium-length, medium-to-high-complexity game. A very different experience each time, depending upon which Ruinous Power you're playing, and I love that there are a lot Of Old World cards but only a quarter of them get used each game.

So far, Nurgle and Slaanesh have won every game I've played in. Khorne has come very close via the dial. Tzeentch is definitely the hardest to play, but I personally find him (her? it?) to be the most fun so far. (I've heard of one of my friends winning as Tzeentch, but I didn't witness it. One Tzeentch player we had fought Nurgle to a tie before losing at the last possible minute.)

I've played once with 3 players: it was fine. But with the intended 4-5 players, it's one of my absolute favorite games, so I'd rather save it for those occasions. If you insist on trying a 3-player game, I recommend leaving out Khorne.

[thing=95619]The Horned Rat[/thing] is a great addition as well, but I wouldn't use it in a 4-player game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Playing evil gods of Chaos is tonnes of fun! This is a theme-heavy game with crazy interactions, built on a solid, elegant base. Controversial points are the high amounts of randomness, unrestricted (but costly) aggression, and dominant playstyle for each side.

Duration: Pretty fast for its kind; there is no building up and players get straight into the struggle. There is some accounting but it's not that long.
Design: Drama and theme atop elegance and balance, with plenty of variety from random events and the different Chaos Powers you can play. The Chaos Powers are have different objectives, types and number of figures, ability cards, and play styles. Games tend to be very close, with lots of directed aggresion and temporary alliances. Ganging-up and the unrestricted potential for hostilities are tempered by a design that tends to make aggression stall a player's progress, and the ambiguity over who is actually in the lead. Unfortunately, there are useless and semi-dominant cards and upgrades; the rules have loose ends and ambiguities, and it's clear that some effects are written different than intended (usually weak Old World cards). Additionally, I've grown a bit tired of the Khorne-management aspect of the game. One major issue is the 3-4 player restriction.
Strategy: Each side has different strengths and weaknesses, and ganging up lasts only a turn or two as the leader is swiftly replaced. There tends to be a particular playstyle for each side but there is enough happening each game that there are many options within that style. So far there doesn't appear to be a dominant side or strategy within a side, partly because of the unrestricted directed aggression.
Components: Reference material contains errors, and rules are ambiguous on many points. Figures are flimsy, board is beatiful but camouflages pieces.
Theme: Brilliant Warhammer Fantasy theme realised in the different sides; Old World especially is abstracted, but still retains character in events.

My nitpicks are:
* Too many useless cards - many upgrades, including all the warrior upgrades will never be used competitively, and many chaos cards are so situational as to be irrelevant;
* Nurgle's dial is impossible rather than implausible;
* Too much resisting Khorne at the beginning;
* Not enough fighting;
* Low threat from Old World.

RATING: 7
Chaos is a successful hybrid between euro and ameritrash. The euro part certainly dominates as it is mainly an area-control game but theme and (small-scale) dice battles play a prominent role as well.

The most distinguishing factors of the game, however, are the asymmetrical player powers and the end-game conditions. Each of the Chaos gods has its own character and respective powers all of which require very different strategies. Despite these differences all gods seem to be balanced very well. Additionally, the game can end on a score of conditions and depending on which end you're working towards you need to emphasise different aspects during the game. They even have an ending where the Old World wins and all Gods lose collectively.

ALSO OWN:

Chaos in the Old World: The Horned Rat Expansion


Man, this is so much fun.  Corrupt the land, purge the villagers, eviscerate the heroes, chaos reigns.

Combat, area control, variable powers, dice-chucking, and lots of cardplay.  The whole 'energy' track where players take turns playing cards, trying not to run out of energy (or else they have to pass and are unable to respond to what opponents do anymore for that round)...it's really a sharp design and one of the best of the year it was released.

I don't like how it stops at 4 players, but the expansion fixes that.

RATING: 7
Fortunately, Chaos in the Old World worked out. There is always some concern about asymmetrical games and especially ones where certain factions are rated by some to be easier to win with than the others. It did have this appearance, but certainly it isn't the case. I do think that some understanding of the game's mechanics are necessary to play it well with any of the factions. Although it's hard to play well when it is your first time (easiest with Khorne though), it is a game that encourages you to play multiple times and learn the different strategies that are available. Overall, a good mix of card play, dice battles and unique player skills and goals. A little clunky for the first time, but turns move relatively quickly. Not having a minimum knowledge of the factions does make for a somewhat chaotic game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[b][i]Chaos in the Old World[/i][/b] is an area control game with asymmetric player powers. The four players are 4 chaos gods of the universe of Warhammer and each god is played very differently. With abilities that are so diverse, it's amazing that the game is balanced. I know nothing about the Warhammer universe or why that particular implement of hand-to-hand fighting is important enough to spawn a franchise, but the different abilities give enough character to each god that I can't help saying "Blood for the blood god!" whenever my red soldiers kill an enemy. The upgrade dials are a fantastic addition and make Chaos in the Old World unique. They also serve to give each god immediate direction and suggest strategies for each player (further differentiating the different gods).

You are the bad guys in this game. You spread evil across a continent and, with cards like Rain of Pus, the game doesn't apologize for this. This will bother some. A more egregious problem (in my opinion) is that the game is not very flexible on the player count. It's amazing with 4 players, okay with 3, and otherwise unplayable.

RATING: 8
What an interesting mix of majority control and pure evil Warhammer goodness. There is a good amount of randomness and it seems like you really have to go with your side's special abilities. Just the same, you are generally rewarded for good play, just be sure to keep everyone in check.

The art is dark, the theme is dark. You are playing the baddies in a very dark game world, so yes, not for sensitive types or kids. If you like the Warhammer world, I'd think you'd enjoy this, unless it ends up being too Euro for you.

I have a hard time finding fault with this game and I can't think of a better dice-rolling majority game. It has strategy, but there is a good amount of variety and I am a sucker for games where each player has unique strengths and weaknesses.

RATING: 3
If you are expecting something exciting where gods really duke it out to taint the old world then you probably want to move along to something else quick.

Instead of pulse pounding game with lots of theme this game might as well be one of those ultra dry area control farming or medieval city building games with cubes. Because that is what our group felt like after playing it.

It has great looking minis, a cool map, a nifty multidial on the board advancement mechanic... but it lacks.... character, excitement, and most of all fun.

Combat is nearly Smallworld dull, and the game seems more of a chore than a game.

Expect nearly zero GW flavor in this game.

If you enjoy area control snoozefests and want to pretend you like trashy games, you will like this game alot.

The only overall positive about this game is that I finally found a game that I can rate a 3.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
hhhhhm, I really can't compare it to anything, risk ;)

It definitely feels AT.

I like the two ways of victory AND one way of 'total' loss.  I think it when a 'non-coop' game puts in a way for all players to lose.

You are basically 'leading' demonic forces that are spreading throughout the land to 'corrupt' the various countries and bring them into 'decay'.  You do that by spreading your 'followers' and they spread corruption.  When a country is corrupted to a certain point it falls into decay and points are awarded.  You get points for just adding corruption, and then the 1st and 2nd place get bonus points.  So, this part feels somewhat like El Grande (or any area control game were it's about placing and not 'killing').  The points are for killing, killing is valid, but at that point it becomes about 'depriving' of points and not about scoring points.

There is, however, a secondary win condition.  Each 'power' has it's own way of 'corrupting' the world.  If they do that in a turn their 'dial' turns.  Who ever does the most to furture their god gets a bonus turn.  If it turns 7 times they win.   Each turn also scores points, bring more things onto the board, or improves your forces.  This also indicates 'your threat level'.  Bad things in countries normally trigger against the forces that represent the highest threat, while the one with the lowest threat gets to choose things when there is a choice.

Also, you get to play 'chaos' cards onto the board which helps you or hurts others.

I'm not sure how balanced it really is.  I think Nurgle might end up being a bit harder to play then the others.

Khorne goal is to attack others and peasants on the board.  So, they can move around to whereever their targets are.  Slannish needs to corrupt where nobles and heroes are, the game starts with quite a few on the board, more might come on during the game AND he has 'Chaos' cards that allow the player to move them where he needs them to be.  Tzeentch needs to corrupt where magic is.  There are a few Warpstones start on the board and a few come on during the game.  But, he also gets the benefit that some of the actions other people do will help him in his goal.  So, he gets some flexibility on where to go.  

Nurgle on the other hand must add corruption to 'populous' states on the board.  There are only something like 4 or 5 of them and they are static.  I think he had a spell which would make one 'populous' but it didn't seem to come out.  But, the goal seemed a lot more 'static' then the others.  That's why I'll need to play it some more to see how balanced it really is.

smell: -------:what:-------

RATING: 9
Wow. It's like el grande slept with my copy of the warhammer fantasy daemon codex?  This is a really flavorful and easy game with surprisingly close balance.  The combination of risk like battles and area majority scoring provides a nice backdrop for the metric shitload of old school warhammer flavor on the chaos cards, the gods' threat dial victory conditions, and the differences in the units.  My only trepidation about the game is that I doubt it plays as well with three.  EDIT: With the horned rat, you can now play with 4 or 5 either, which helps some.  It plays OK with three, but is far superior with 4.

A lot of the balance comes from the players watching what the others are doing.  That isn't necessarily a bad thing and is hard to avoid in a game where there is a lot of interaction, like this one.  One great thing about this game I didn't appreciate the first time is how fast it is.  It truly is about a 90 minute game, but it feels like a big epic longer game.

RATING: 6

RATING: 8
This one takes a few rounds to get your head around, but I liked it. It did seem like it was possible to jet from first to last and vice versa, but perhaps the players could work together to stop this kind of thing. In fact, that may be the biggest drawback I see: players really need to be aware of everything their opponents are trying to accomplish, and this isn't easy with so many victory conditions and player powers, especially the first time through. However, it definitely seemed at times like all four players had a shot at winning at times, and there are lots of interesting choices to make. 

UPDATE: Came back to this one a few years later and found some of the same issues, but mostly a solid game. I really feel that Eric Lang's more recent effort, Blood Rage, does what this game wants to do only better. Granted, if you are a fan of having a particular strategic path assigned to you rather than drafting one, you may prefer CitOW, but I really feel Blood Rage is better in every way.

RATING: 10
This is a deep assymetrical area control game with much character. Players play evil gods competing against each other to destroy humankind. The rules are simple but the gameplay is deep. The reason for this is that there are three scoring mechanisms: domination and ruination (each of which scores you VPs) and threat (dial advancement). You need to keep track of how everyone is doing on each of these levels, and make careful decisions accordingly. For example, it often pays to make short term sacrifices to prevent another player from making long term gains. Given the fluidity of the game this is easier said than done...and it can be difficult for new players to play at the level of the scoring rather than at the level of their plastic figures only. In other words, players should expect to stumble a few times as they figure out how to read the game and see enough of how it is developing that they can make well-timed interventions to help their own position and stop another player from winning. For these reasons I advise against playing this game with people who lack the focus to play a deep game, or who are easily discouraged.

Downtime is low, there is much direct conflict and interaction among players, there is a tremendous amount of thematic flavour, and the artwork and components are very well done.  See my [url=http://fortressat.com/index.php?option=com_content&task=view&id=1466&Itemid=551]understated review at Fortress Ameritrash [/url] for a few more details.

[b]3 player game[/b]: With only 3 players, there is a bit more breathing space. The balancing act of the gameplay (where hindering the progress of other players ia just as important as advancing your own position) is a bit easier. It isn't as tense, but it still worth playing, and it is a good way to introduce the game to people who do not normally play boardgames.
