RATING: 7
When I first heard of this, I was turned off with the supposed theme. I vehemently dislike horror games--with it's emphasis on evil, and words like "Chaos" in the title, this smelled like another stupid zombie game. And being from FFG, the folks that brought you the nightmare that is Arkham Horror, well, I was more than reluctant to play this... but I had no choice; there were four of us, and as soon as this hit the table, the two newbies in the gaming group hit on it like vultures on a corpse.

Boy, was I surprised. Set in an alternate Warhammer 40K universe, you are in control of one of four demonic factions battling it out to corrupt the Old World. Each faction (actually power) has asymmetric powers, with a specialty that is reflected in its force mix, action cards, and game objectives. After awhile, this reminded me of Risk:2210 AD--area control, limited number of turns, special objectives, cards, and specialized units, except set in a fantasy setting. The game is not only about amassing units and moving around the board attacking and "killing" (which my faction specialized in and was rewarded for), but also laying down 'chaos' cards which can affect the play within region they are placed in. Whoever is able to amass enough units exceeding some threshold (unique for each region), gets VP equal to the threshold at the end of each turn (of six). Also, if they have cultists in the mix, they get to 'corrupt' the region. When a region gets twelve corruption tokens, players with the most corruption get mega VPs, and the region is out play for the rest of the game. All players, as a total, must corrupt at least five regions on the board by the end, or the game wins, so there is a slight cooperative aspect. Another novel feature is that each power has a status dial (kind of like from BSG), which increments as each power satisfies a unique condition of theirs, thereby getting upgrades and improved capabilities to their forces. (It's interesting to note that more games are starting to incorporate 'dials' directly into the board, e.g., BSG, Chicago Express. This must be a new trend, but it can be annoying as it takes up valuable table/board space.)

The components are standard FFG (which means lots of tiny counters), with thick cardboard, great graphics, and fantastic, yet flimsy, plastic figures. There aren't too many rules, and the list of actions each player can take is limited, but the strategic and tactical decisions are many. I agree with one of the reviewers that the board regions are tiny for the given number of units (and cards).

This is a surprisingly good game, albeit with a sinister, dark theme. If one is able to get into character, it's a rumpus good time, and the truly asymmetric powers/capabilities (we're talking nasty, one-sided, unfair) bring about surprises and upsets, as well as engaging gameplay that should remain fresh for a long time. Initial rating: '8'.

Update (09/27/11): Haven't played this since that first time, and only a few individuals have it. I don't think I'll go out of my way to get this. Downgrading it to a '7'.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

UPDATE:  After four "learning" games—separated by enough time to make the rules hazy, and with different players at each session—it's clear to me that this is only going to shine with complete knowledge of the various powers, and especially the contents of their decks.

On the one hand this suggests a huge amount of replay value—it would take at least four plays (once as each power) just to get to the starting point of deeper understanding—but in reality it's going to be difficult to string together connected plays with the same people in a culture where very few are interested in wearing a game out.

But I love the idea of this, the mythos and presentation, and so I must try...

RATING: 10

Coupled with beautiful game pieces and you have a game that is as stunning to watch as it is to play.

If you didn't know the Warhammer back-story of the Ruinous powers, you will after this game. Aptly captures the bickering nature of Chaos gods and the carnage they spill into the mortal world in their timeless quarrel.

RATING: 4
A ghastly Ameritrash game that depends entirely on the players understanding and monitoring what each of the others needs to do in order to win. Where players have different experience levels then the system crashes and burns completely. For example: Tzeentch scores for having two cultists in a region with two stones, Khorne gets points just for killing them (thereby both scoring and preventing Tzeentch from scoring!). So, who stops Khorne? The final nail in the coffin for me is that each of the factions is 'evil' and therefore it's impossible to sympathise with its objectives, so losing the attractive narrative that's an essential part of experience games. Not one for me.

I'm two plays in. And right now, I'm pleasantly happy with my purchase. I think it packs an amazing amount of unique situations into a smooth package.

The four gods play drastically different. I played the control god super well and won as him. I played nurgle horribly and lost for it. But I want to play it again and play them *right*! I think that's the sign of a great game.

It's not that hard to explain. And the turns flow fast and the game wraps up before you know it.

I don't like some of the classic "stop him, he's going to win", but that's just the type of game it is, you can't actually avoid it.

The theme may turn some off, but I actually find it unique and refreshing. We constantly say we're "Pooping" corruption all over the map. The balance of Dial turns and Victory Points is just savory.

Jul10:  So once we got better at the gameplay... it turned into "Whack the leader."  That's not the type of game I actually enjoy all that much.

RATING: 7.5
This is the 2nd time I have seen Eric take an old design and redux it (see his Mystic game and AGOT-TCG) into something cool as to me this is Midgard revamped, rethemed and given a steroid injection for good measure.

Its a very cool AC variable power system with 4 different players potentially following different paths to win. There are lots of things to ponder and track here but I do share the concerns that you simply have to play to the power you are playing to win, its not like Khorne is going to win on VP's here. So while each one is different you likely have to play to its strengths to win limiting your viable "options" on how to play. So while with most good multiple path games you can choose to play different game each time here you will but only if you play a different faction (this is a very broad statement/generalization and I still do think there is some wiggle room inside your path like upgrades etc however see next sentence). More to this end I also shares John's concern that Slaanesh and Tzenteech may be more interesting to play overall than the other 2 factions which have an even more obvious path, more play is needed to know for sure if any of these concerns are true and how much. Even if true (depending how much), I still enjoy this kind of game and would enjoy testing out each faction and learning how to play well with each one and games will still feel somewhat different each time as it will depend on just how each player approaches the game. More to that end, it is [i]imperative[/i] that everyone at the table track each others progress and act accordingly, much more so than many other games. 

It seems they really did a good job with the theme. I also really liked the 2 card limit per territory and this added some tension/push your luck and made some decisions tough. Probably only going to play great with 4 though the expansion means you can play 5. I did find a little chaos (and luck) actually, not sure how much it bothers me yet but I think things are likely a little harder to control with 5 than 3 or 4.

RATING: 9
The whole proves better than the sum of its parts - I wouldn't have thought I'd end up liking an area control game with heavy doses of take-that gameplay.

The variable player powers make the game though, as well as keeping the take-that in check.  Since the players have fairly well-defined objectives take-that play is limited to getting someone out of one's way or trying to stop the win.  The game also doesn't overstay its welcome; we saw five ruinations very quickly.

There are some very interesting game-theoretical questions that this game has raised both times I've played it - mainly concerning the tightrope Tzeentch and Slaanesh have to walk keeping Khorne and Nurgle in check.  Definitely on my "games I need to play more" list.

RATING: 9
Chaos in the Old World has a special place in my heart for being one of the first games that I bought "on my own" in the early years of my hobby. It's among the first (and still most significant) steps on my way to establishing a boardgaming identity of my own.

But that's not why it's a 9/10. The reason is that it's a really good, solid game. It's dripping with theme, the presentation is top notch, player interaction is furious, the pacing is excellent and it's generally really well balanced, although the randomized setups tend to favor some player roles more than others in each game. At its best, the game is extremely tight, usually with two players desperately struggling for victory neck and neck, both of them equally plausible winners at the beginning of what will turn out to be the last round of the game.

It's worth owning the Horned Rat expansion, because playing the game with the Morrslieb decks has a very different feel and role balance from the base game. I can't say which is "better", but that's probably a good thing, since neither deck feels like a downgrade from the other.

RATING: 7
MG
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Euro salad tossed in an Ameritrash dressing.

Chaos in the Old World is a majority game where scoring occurs on three levels: short term (domination), long term (corruption) and [i]cumulative[/i] (threat level). A simple Kramer-style action point allowance system provides the engine. Add to these very euro-style game design elements combat, variable player powers, random events, upgrades, and a hand of spell/influence cards and then you have a "special action" majority game (a la El Grande) taken to an extreme level. Although Kramer is not listed as a designer, this feels very much like a collaboration he might have done with Eric Lang.

And somehow, it all works! It is a beautiful, glorious, and thoroughly depraved mess of a game, while being quite lean by FFG standards. I am not a big fan of what we call Ameritrash games - the die rolling is tedious and most themes are meaningless to me - but one thing that I do appreciate are variable player powers that lead to variable strategies between players. I think this is one of the reasons I like Agricola so much, that the cards help to define a unique strategy that no other player will be pursuing in quite the same way. Well, color me surprised, but Chaos scratches a very similar itch for me that Agricola does. This is a game that truly bridges the gap between European- and American-style game designs.

As with all FFG games, this is hard to confidently rate after a single play because it takes time to figure out how balanced all of the special powers and cards are. It is definitely my favorite FFG game to date.

RATING: 7
The idea of CitOW really appeals to me: direct conflict, assymetrical powers. Every Chaos God plays differently, and for the game to be satisfying each player has to play close attention to the shifting balance of power. 

After one play, I can rate the game a solid seven with potential to go to an 8. I played as Khorne initially, and Khorne seems like a great idea in terms of game design (constantly attacking the other players, particularly the ones momentarily in good positions) but in practice he seems kind of boring to play. I'm not sure Nurgle would be much better - it really seems like the intriguing sides of the game are Slaanesh and Tzenteech. In a game with two potential runaways and two "back door victory" characters, the latter seems inherently more interesting to play. Might just be me. 

The gameplay seems functional but perhaps not more than that. Unit placement, movement, and differentiation meet minimum standards. Ditto cardplay: interesting but only just. And that might be fine once players reach a certain level of familiarity. If you can focus exclusively on the shifting balance of power and use the simple tools provided by the game, maybe it's pretty good. 

Best FFG game in a long time. In fact, probably one of their only good games. 

Update: after a second play, I'm not sure I see long-term prospects here. Maybe there's just too much system-fallout in this one for me. I'm starting to realize that I really dislike games that "check" various statuses and pull the associated tripwires.

RATING: 8

**Update** 
Plays very well - 90 minutes tops. After some confusion with Khornes dial advancement condition the game seems to be very well balanced. Each power plays very differently and all players need to be aware of the other players in order to keep everyone in check.

*Update** Has stood up to repeated play - i'm still rubbish at it though!

**Update 29th Oct 09** Like this so much I got El Grande in a trade (which also looks exciting after a rules read).

I Like: 
*Variable player powers mean a new game every time.
*Varied strategies for each power - last game Khorne was 3VP off a VP win!
*Plays very well with 3 or 4 players, which surprised me.
*Great theme!
*Play time of just over an hour with 3 players.
*Having your own deck of cards to play - I really like this mechanic.

Dislike:
*The board is poorly laid out. This has stopped bothering me due to familiarity but 1st couple of games was a definite issue.
*This is the type of game where someone who doesn't 'play their own game' can arbitrarily ruin someone elses game.

RATING: 8
Wonderfully asymmetric, easy to teach and plays in a reasonable amount of time.  A good solid design with a lot to offer.  Everyone's always been able to pick the game up fast when I've taught it.  Primary downside for me is it shares the typical multi-player emphasis on trying to make sure you're not the one in the cross hairs when it comes time to kill the leader.  Also, there is a whole layer of meta-strategy which will not be apparent to players until they are more familiar with the decks and dials possessed by each of the ruinous powers.

RATING: 7
The Warhammer universe couldn't be further away from my gaming preferences but I enjoyed this game. Sure, it is multiplayer conflict, dice can play a decisive role and it depends on players keeping each other in check. But it strikes the right balance with area majority scoring, strategic possibilities and tactical optimizations to make it work as a Weuro - a genre I usually don't enjoy too much (Antike or Struggle of Empires, to name games where the balance isn't right for me).
In my one game I played Khorn who feels like the odd one out being the only faction who doesn't need to corrupt something on the board and just has to kill others. Coupled with the playstyle of my group that they only fought against Khorn and expected Khorn to do the dirty work of fighting to keep all others in balance at the same time, this isn't the best faction to play.

RATING: 9
This is as close to the perfect asymmetric area control game as I've ever seen.  It's also the only game in my collection that's almost always fun whether you win or lose because if you're on the losing end you can still "do stuff" and affect the outcome.  After playing too many "down and out" games, this was refreshing.

I'd have given this a 10 but for one issue:  the whole experience is so taut, dependent on all players to play their sides well, that games involving players with varying skill levels can often end up skewed; this often means you can play really well but still end up behind the 8-ball.  Chaos, it certainly is.

RATING: 6
I thought this would be kind of nutty, but it's really pretty much an orderly, Euroish game with admittedly some decent take-that. The board is horrific, and not in the "Oh, my God, is that human skin?" but in the "Oh, shit, I can't really figure out what links to what." The text on the tiny cards is printed on an artsy background, which made it almost impossible for me to read in modest light. Gameplay is easy enough to get down, there's some OK asymmetry, and at least our game was fairly close. (I ended up missing a VP win by one point, then ended up third the next turn.) I can't say I really felt like a chaos god, though; I felt more like an optimizer. Some complaints were heard among my fellow players about a lack of balance in the gods, which I can't attest to myself, and I doubt I'll play it enough to ever find out--though I wouldn't turn down a game if asked and there were no obvious alternatives.

RATING: 9.9
Chaos w starym świecie to jedna z najlepszych dostępnych na rynku gier strategicznych. Jest to jedna z moich ulubionych gier. Wykonanie jest świetne, jedynym minusem są "słoneczka" kultystów, które prędzej czy później odczepią się. Mimo to gra odwdzięcza się wspaniałymi emocjami przy stole. Rywalizacja potęg jest wyczuwalna, a fakt, że fabularnie bogowie chaosu nienawidzą się, wyklucza współpracę :-) Mimo to negatywna interakcja nie boli. Fakt, że z rozgrywki na rozgrywkę, gracze stają się bardziej świadomi swoich (bóstw) mocnych i słabszych stron. Balans gry jest wręcz nieprawdopodobny, każda potęga ma realne szanse na zwycięstwo. Losowość, która w grze występuje w walce (rzuty kośćmi), dociągu kart chaosu, oraz wydarzeniach, nie jest dokuczliwa. Fakt, czasami dane wydarzenie daje korzyści jednemu z bóstw, ale nie są to często korzyści, których nie da się zablokować. Złożoność zasad jest pozorna, nowi gracze, którym tłumaczę grę przez 10-20 minut, załapują sens już w 2 rundzie. Grę gorąco polecam!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Sin duda, uno de los mejores juegos que ha visto la luz últimamente: la temática es potente y mucho más motivadora que criar cerdos o mover cubitos. Los materiales son una gozada: el tablero es precioso en su macabra concepción, las cartas texturizadas, las minis cumplen con su cometido y, sin ser maravillosas, son bastante decentes. Las ruedas del Caos, flipantes. Las peculiares características  de cada dios y sus distintos caminos para conseguir la victoria hacen que jugar con cada uno de los Poderes del Caos sea una experiencia de juego absolutamente distinta. Y más aún: la forma aleatoria en que las fichas de piedra, nobles, héroes y demás se distribuyen al comienzo de la partida pueden también forzar estrategias distintas;  y el hecho de usar siete cartas de entre todas las disponibles (28) para determinar los distintos eventos que tendrán lugar a lo largo de la partida, hacen que cada partida no tenga nada que ver con la anterior, ni aún jugándola con el mismo dios.

Por tanto: rejugabilidad brutal, con temática atrayente, interacción y puteo, estrategia, poco azar = JUEGAZO.

RATING: 5
4*****
---
A suprisingly dull game. Looking at the box/theme/board/minis you should be expecting a good fun fight filled little game but... well, maybe it is for 1 of the players. the other 3 are playing a very simple but long and fiddly euro game. 

If you play the red guy - attack the 'leader' and try to roll high. If you are the other 3 - avoid the red guy and try to convince him that someone else is winning.

I've played this a few times and others really like it but i must admit it doesn't do a lot for me - seems like a game that does a few different things but doesn't do any of them very well. Also, the end turns into one of the worse cases of everyone saying "right, we all need to stop xxxx or he will win next turn"...

RATING: 6.5
I have no knowledge of the Warhammer universe, but in the end it didn't seem necessary to enjoy the game.

The pieces are standard FFG: high quality and beautiful imagery. It takes a bit to get used to the directional arrows, but they trace out a path that is identical every game, so eventually you just know where they point.

The interactive and strategic level of the game is quite enjoyable. You only get a limited number of points to deploy either troops or ability cards. Cultist troops cannot battle enemy troops but they can corrupt the board, which can net you huge points if you're persistent.

Having a point-based victory condition is all well and fine, but FFG, staying true to form, added a non-point victory condition for each player, as well as a way that all players can lose. This feature alone makes the game extremely interesting. Also, having a feature that gives an edge to the player in last place is a mechanic I can get behind.

Just about the only problem I have with this game is the level of randomness associated with the battle dice (and also how they only include 5 dice in the set), but sometimes these can make for the best stories afterward.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
To summarize the concept of this game I would say that it is El Grande with cooler stuff. Its basically an area of majority game with more special abilities and neat things.

It is not horrible but there are a few things I don't like.

First, you have so few cards in your hands, I would have preferred to draw 3 or 4 cards every turn instead of 2. More cards is not bad for the game considering you have to pay for them and that there is a limited amount that can be placed on the board.

Second, the dials are interesting but many effects are useless. Gaining power ups that way is OK and ingenious. but some levels only makes you game VP or remove tokens from the board. Totally useless. Give be bonus troops, bonus corruptions, more mana, but actually give me something worth leveling up.

Third, the map is very hard to read. Yes it is cool, but there are too much colors and it is hard to identify the stuff on the board. It is even hard to see the borders of the regions. So I really think they should have redesigned the colors of the map.

If you love area majority games with some variety and replay value, that might be the game for you.

---------------------------------------------------------------------

So even if I have a lot of issue with this game, it is the best game in it's category. So if you like this kind of game, you might just take this one.

RATING: 6
LIKE:

* The asymmetrical abilities of the four different evil gods as well the different set of cards for each leads to interesting conflict and pursuit of two different victory conditions.
* Having a couple different viable winning conditions keeps you on your toes and you must work together to keep the leaders down in their respective categories.
* Corruption is an interesting piece to bring about ruination and potentially more points.  
* Heroes, nobles, magic, and other chits bring in a variable starting board and an everchanging board.
* Despite the fact that my forces were continually destroyed by a hot hand with dice, I do like that aspect that you hit with and reroll and die that rolls a 6...giving you potential to destroy a lot of opponents.  

DISLIKE:

* I have a strong aversion to the dark theme and art of this game.  My preference would be a different setting/context.
* I'm not certain dice rolls will even out over the course of a game.  This is a game that needs this to occur, but I can live with dice, just can be frustrating.
* Can't say I'm a fan of having the attacker decide what he destroys from defensive forces.  However, that might be necessary in this game to maintain game balance.  Still, it just grates me the wrong way.

20/10/2010

RATING: 9
"Your shrines will burn, your streets run with blood, your false idols shattered, your people slaughtered by the thousands, your very planet torn apart… and the barest fraction of my hatred will be satisfied." sums up the games theme. :D
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
My one game of this lasted 3 hours but I am assuming that is usually not the case, and so my rating reflects if this game is normally 2 hours or less (otherwise this goes down to a 6).

I definitely see a lot of things in this game that were later implemented in Blood Rage: action points with you taking one action per turn until you are out of action points with the points being used to deploy units and play various cards, having possible upgrades to your action points and units, area control, areas of the board that get blown up, order of how you perform your actions being very important, plays best with 4, possibly have runaway leader problem, and very different ways you can win.

Difference between players: The greatest strength of CitOW compared to Blood Rage is definitely the assymetric factions that all play very differently. The red race wants to kill enemy units and has the easiest time winning via fulfilling their track, green likes to spread around the board and likes to win via VPs, purple mind controls opposing units and can win via either way, and blue has a much stronger access to their utility cards and also can win either way. While I'm not sure if it has as much replayability as the Blood Rage draft (where even if you go for a general strategy you can draft slightly different cards each round), I felt much more different than Blood Rage would even if players drafted very different play styles.

Units: I like how each unit has different stats in Chaos in the Old World for different players. While I think technically you have more eventual customization options in Blood Rage, the immediate difference and the special powers being more special in CitOW still makes it win this category for me.

Area control: It is much easier to move around in Blood Rage as you can move a group at a time, and don't have to deploy adjacent to previous units. This also means in Blood Rage you can't get stuck in a corner as easily with a difficult time to fight your way through to spread across the board. However I think this makes positioning more important in CitOW especially with the added counters on the board, and also I like how you both need to consider corruption tokens and also #units + cards played in each region rather than just overall strength as in Blood Rage.

Combat: This is where Blood Rage shines the best. The single card combat is both clean and strategic. In comparison, the large amounts of die rolling in CitOW is fairly luck based and has a bit of downtime. While in theory it is cool to have the slightest of chances your single unit can wipe out a huge army, overall CitOW's combat system seems dated.

Progression: I think Blood Rage has a significantly better progression, as while you start more similar there are more avenues you can expand with the different cards each round. This is despite the greater control of what region blows up in CitOW and the event cards being different each time gives a sort of board progression in that game.

Victory Conditions: CitOW seemingly has much greater difference in its victory conditions as in Blood Rage you can only win via points, but the thing is both the wheel and VPs generally still require you to get area control, and so you still strive for the same general objective. In comparison, in Blood Rage the Loki cards encourage you to actively lose by spreading yourself thin, there are some stat boosting cards that incentivize you to be off on your lonesome to poach pillage rewards, and there is the general strategy of getting into the thick of fights.

Thematic Flair vs. Streamlining: This I think should overall be what ultimately decides which game you should get. There are much more unique cards in CitOW whereas a lot of cards in Blood Rage are just variations of each other or are pretty generic (e.g. increased strength, yellow vs. blue region quests, generic stat boosts). However, Blood Rage's generic nature also makes the flow of play much more smooth as while there are still tough decisions each turn, the ramifications of each are less convoluted. It also makes Blood Rage quite a bit easier to learn I believe. Overall, while I really like the assymmetry and thematic flair of CitOW, I like Blood Rage more because of its streamlined nature, superior battle system, and the fact that I prefer Norse mythology to a random made up one (but thank you Eric Lang for still making more mythology games, creating a cool universe, and not doing another Greek mythology one).

RATING: 9.2
This game is my gateway game. I played it over 50 times, often with the same people.
It's really amazing - you get to be the Chaos Gods from Warhammer Fantasy and you get to destroy the Old World. The game is asymmetric and offers two different ways to achieve victory.
The main weakness of this game is that it becomes unbalanced if one or more players are inexperienced. You can still enjoy it, but for a tight game everybody should have played 3+ games.
Should you however get that veteran gaming group together you can expect extremely tight games, where typically 3 out of 4 players have a chance to win in the last round.

RATING: 5
This is a terrible game for anyone who doesn't want to have a cutthroat PVP experience from the get go.  Additionally if there is anyone at the table who has more experience with the game, and gods forbid you have a new player or two in the mix ... the game is going to suck.  Once players know all the cards in the four factional decks and have five or six games under the game gets better. 

It just doesn't have enough variability to be worth playing over and over.  I can see it being a game you play several times to learn, then a few times once you really have the strategy of the game down, then get bored quickly only playing it once or twice a year after that point.  There is a single expansion for it with really probably no potential to expand it more beyond that. 

I'd recommend it to any Warhammer Fantasy fans out there who don't happen to own it yet, but if one isn't in that camp there is no reason to buy this pretty forgettable game.  The only reason it has as high a ranking as it does here on boardgame geek is the legions of GW fanboys who blindly snap this stuff up and then gush about it no matter how good or bad it is.

Chaos in the Old World makes you a god. Each god’s distinctive powers and legion of followers grant you unique strengths and diabolical abilities with which to corrupt and enslave the Old World.
    Khorne, the Blood God, the Skulltaker, lusts for death and battle.
    Nurgle, the Plaguelord, the Father of Corruption, luxuriates in filth and disease.
    Tzeentch, the Changer of Ways, the Great Conspirator, plots the fate of the universe.
    Slaanesh, the Prince of Pleasure and Pain, the Lord of Temptations, lures even the most steadfast to his six deadly seductions.
Yet, as you and your fellow powers of Chaos seek domination by corruption and conquest, you must vie not only against each other, but also against the desperate denizens of the Old World who fight to banish you back to the maelstrom of the Realm of Chaos.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Eric Lang is a designer whose involvement in a game always makes me sit up and take notice, even with projects like Quarriors that would not have been of that much interest to me without him on board. A game built around the chaotic gods of the Old World, one of my favorite aspects of the Warhammer Fantasy background, would have been enticing enough without him, but became an insta-buy once I spotted his name in the previews.

And the game did not disappoint and continues to be one of my favorites from recent years. The core mechanic of an area majority game where the contested areas are destroyed by the players as they go along is both a delightful fit thematically and an interesting new strategic dynamic. Putting individual victory conditions on top of that cake increases the thematic depth and the asymmetry of the four factions, and additionally prevents the game from becoming stale by providing different ways to the top of the god heap.

The many interconnections of cultists, areas and the chaos wheels are a delight once you’ve sorted them out, but they cause one of the two slight problems with the game: it sets up a rather fragile balance that can be tipped by a single inattentive or inexperienced (or, possibly, plain dumb) player. Keeping the other powers in check is sometimes more important than advancing your own plans, and this can be difficult to wrap your head around. The other problem of only working properly with exactly four players is forgivable and partly remedied by the Horned Rat expansion, which allows rousing five player games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5

RATING: 9
A great game set in the Warhammer Fantasy universe wherein up to four players (five with the expansion) play the Gods of Chaos battling for dominion in the war-ridden lands of the Old World.
Whether you're playing the evasive Slaanesh, the unpredictable Tzeentch, the in-your-face Khorne, or the contagious Nurgle makes a huge difference in how you're going to play the game, with different units, upgrades, and power cards all unique to each faction, and players will find themselves enjoying the game more or less according to which power they incarnate. This is where the game really shines in my opinion, as people in the same game group will find their preferred playstyle and will also learn what their enemies can do, making the game incredibly deep and strategic.

RATING: 8
4 / 5 - Very Good

Very good indeed, with a fantastic balance of strategy and (appropriately enough) chaos. It's fairly (not completely) balanced but has such huge asymmetry in the player powers that this is actually a fairly impressive achievement. It's a highly procedural game that has to be followed to the letter, which is kind of a bind, but it's difficult, exciting, competitive and tense and the theme sucks you in to the point where it can feel marginally uncomfortable when you start ruining regions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I have a biased perspective, as my view of this game suffers from a comparison to Blood Rage, which I see as a vastly superior game.

While I like the theme, the idea of strongly asymmetric powers, and the nice domination/corruption/ruin means of gaining points, most of the mechanisms disappointed me to some degree:

1) Dice combat is super swingy here, with the extra rolls on 6s, few diced used in combat, and each roll being critical.

2) Random card draws from only about 8 different cards gives little variety, particularly when many of the cards (and upgrades) are situational or almost useless.

3) Asymmetric game play is nice in theory, but you still need a way to have multiple paths to victory, which this game mostly doesn't have. Lang tried with the VP vs. dial advancement thing, but let's face it, Khorne doesn't win on VP and Nurgle doesn't win on the dial. The asymmetry here makes the game very scripted.

4) Card play is very take that - it's not fun or creative.

All this combines to create a game that requires you to play a scripted, bash the leader game, where the winner is the person who either rolls best or gets the least take that cards thrown at them. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Asymmetrical, card driven, action points, dice combat, area control, random event cards, miniatures. All things that make up the game, but they don't really describe what the game is all about. I started with asymmetrical because that is what the game really is. You have Khorne, the only player who really wants to use his warriors (a failing  of the game, but that's a point I'll get to) and will win or lose through combat alone (he's the madman everyone is running away from / trying to lock away in some remote corner / send elsewhere). You have Tzeentch, who wants to mess about, do wierd things to screw over his opposition with fun cards that teleport, mess up those numbers you so carefully counted up, and just simply be the "lord of change" that he is in the fluffy world of Warhammer, unlike Khorne Tzeetch can try to win two very different ways and is trying to avoid choosing until late in the game. Then you have Nurgle, having a filthy party with his cheap horde, what he wants the most is just to camp out in two regions, but he can't cause Khorne is out for blood - he can like Khorne only win one way effectively, by building up to a great turn where th board collapses into all the points he needs to win. Then you have Slaanesh, who like Tzeetch can win two ways and is also trying to wait to decide between the two, his cards are less wacky than Tzeetchs, but he's subtly and innocuously strengthening his position until he just emerges as the winner by default.

So say you are Tzeetch, and you really want to play your cool warrior creature and go tangle with that bastard Nurgle, well you can't... And if you do well, thanks, Khorne won game over...

The game is much more confined in the gamespace than what emerges from the rules, really, there are so many things that you just can't do because it means a) You'll lose but even worse b) You give the game away (usually to Khorne). This is the strongest negative I have of the game. It's fragile when players don't invest enough to play their part "correctly". However, with players who understand games and can with some insight judge which actions are "candidate moves" as opposed to "give the game to player X moves" (and really, it isn't that hard) - the game becomes a tense affair where everyone is screwing with everyone, trying to jab, push and pull their way to victory while also fulfilling their "job" in the game (that is, preventing everyone else from victory).

It's a game that allows you to be clever, with comboing cards and creatures, relying on your opposition to do your job for you so you can spend your resources getting ahead instead. So if everyone does their part right, the game shines. If you play with mixed company, with someone who does things "just because", or similar, it falls apart and becomes frustrating (like many multiplayer games with direct interaction between players), so don't play it with those!

A great game!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Played once, with three people who had played multiple times before.  

Players play as one of four races fighting for control over eight-or-so regions in the world.  Players score points for controlling regions and being the first to destroy a region with corruption tokens.  Players can also win without victory points by advancing a "dial".  

I don't know what it was, but I just didn't get this game.  It's got some area-control aspects, some wargame aspects, and a whole bunch of cardgame "take that" card aspect to it.  The last bit is probably what annoyed me the most: each player has multiple decks that give them special powers in a region for one turn, making predicting what's going to happen in a region insane.

The game is also battle heavy, and units are fairly easy to kill (most require only 1 hit, which is a 50% chance with each attack).  This makes maintaining units in a region tough, yet maintaining a region seems key to victory (to get dominance points and to get corruption points).  Actually, maintining units in a region is only tough if other players pay attention to you: those players who were quietly in a small region could hold it for the whole game because nobody went in; those who were in the center got trashed.  And, of course, my color required me to go into the center to advance my wheel.

The game also took something like three hours or more, including rules, and I just don't think it's predictable enough for that time.  Sure, take-that is fun for an hour, but for three?

Long story short, I think I'm just not a fan of the highly-assymetrical power games that involve a high degree of random combat and have a bunch of "take that" special power cards that can be combo'd to oblivion.

RATING: 9

Update: since purchasing this and playing it a bit more, I can say I am deeply impressed by how... ah fuck it: here's the word for it: [u]elegant[/u] the whole game is. It just flows smoothly, there is never down-time, emotionally, the game feels extremely intense, it plays just as well with three as it does with four, and finally it plays so quickly, that in almost all cases, upon finishing, we all want to immediately play it again. ----------

Update:
Not only is this game hitting the table at almost every gaming session, but after finishing the first game of it, we all look at each other and nod and say, "again." And after several more plays, things are really starting to balance themselves out - we are starting to see far fewer dial click victories and there is a knife fight for VP. There is also some subtlety and strategic depth being revealed in the strange way some of the cards combo together or in how the players move around Khorne.

This is the first game in years that my excitement for has not fizzled with repeated plays.

RATING: 7
A unique, assymetrical game which carries the concept of area control into a completely new level. It is incredibly thematic and requires a lot of thinking and effort to second-guess your opponents' malevolent plans. Each chaos power has a considerably different play style and strategic options abound at every step. On top of that, the number of regions isn't that large, thus with space so tight and four angry gods unleashing their fury with each and every action, the gameplay is always tense and full of surprises. However, if you play the game with people who don't necessarily understand its nuances, then you might just as well get a completely different experience. They'll say that the game is out of balance, and that  Khorne has it all too easy. Right... Just don't listen to that bullshit and get a proper group to play the game - it'll knock your socks off!

EDIT: I've been playing with the Horned Rat expansion for quite a while now and I must say that I'm rather disappointed with the changes it has introduced. I liked the original game better, even though there were some tiny balance issues (which assymetrical game hasn't got those?). What I mean is that although I still like the game in general, then the more I play with the expansion, the less I enjoy the whole experience. Time to move on to something else...

RATING: 8
This is a very good conquest game and one of the only ones I have enjoyed enough to want to play again!

The individual play styles and goals of the different powers sets CitOW apart from the usual Risk-style crap.

The problems keeping it from a 9 and even 10 is that the game group chiefly decides the strength of the gameplay and how enjoyable the experience is.

The fact it must be played with 4 players and 4 players only means it doesn't hit the table as often as it deserves. The balance and effects of the 4 Powers coupled with their play styles keep the game balanced. With one God omitted or poorly played by the owning player one or more of the others will dominate the game and have a much easier time.

Also playing with 4 (or 5 with expansion) usually makes CitOW a long affair- usually 2 hours or more each game. We've had games go to 3 and a half hours with 5 players!

Oh and besides the ability to play with 5 players instead of 4 the expansion sucks.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A really well balanced game that is something of a Euro/Ameritrash hybrid in the way it plays.

Each race worships a different god in the Old World and they all have different means for achieving victory as they look to corrupt the Old World in order to give power to their great one. 

The production values of this one are excellent and the inclusion of race based card decks and victory wheels in addition to a VP track make each race feel very different. 

In fact a player will not do well by playing to a generic strategy here. They must really get to grips with what their race does well and play to those strengths. 

What makes the game tricky at first is knowing how to play your race well but also knowing what other players are trying to do and making sure that you make it hard for them to acheive those goals.

For this reason I think CitOW really begins to shine when a group of 4-5 players reach their 5th plus game as it will all begin to click and flow well then. 

Oh the max player count of 4 for the base game is probably most desireable. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
This is a 7 without the expansion, but an 8 with it. Either way, this is a fantastic area control game. It really does feel like the predecessor to Blood Rage, but while Blood Rage is a quick skirmish for points, this game feels more like a campaign. Blood Rage may be the better design, but I do like a few things about this game a great deal in comparison. I find the card play to be very interesting, the different factions fun to explore, and the event deck is something I like in almost any game it's featured in. At the same time, it's much longer in comparison, and you can't really tailor your team the way you want it to be. It also feels just a tad underdeveloped with how rigid the various strategies are. Still, this is a fantastic game, and is second only to Dominant Species when it comes to epic, multiplayer area control games.

UPDATE: As much as I enjoy Chaos in the Old World, Blood Rage is going to have more staying power due to its varying strategies from game to game. The various factions here are all interesting to explore, but don't offer much in the way of variation...even with the alternate expansion decks.

RATING: 7
Have played three times now spread over two GenCons.  The game is very compact in its rules, which is something that I like.  Each of the 4 "forces" has it's own unique style to win, and it's fairly obvious what that style is.  One force advances by brute fighting.  Another advances by placing corruption in specific areas.  Etc.  Since everything is so up-front, though, everyone has a good sense of everyone else's main objectives.  This produces an atmosphere of attrition, but avoids screwage.  Despite the former, games clock in at 1 to 1.5 hours.  Combine with a very quick set up, and you've got an enjoyable game.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
In this 3-4 player game, each player is an evil race, with their own special abilities. Players play cards or monsters onto the board to earn points or leave corruption on the board. Battles are resolved by rolling dice. A player can win two ways: by scoring 50 pts, or moving their special dial marker around a number of steps.

It's a decent game, where you decide whether you put on weak monsters or strong monsters, and balance it out with cards. You also have a choice to score VPs, or try to get your dial around to win. Looking forward to more plays. It does take a couple plays to understand the rules, nuances and strategy. Playing with an inexperienced player who just feeds Khorne, for example, doesn't bode well for the other players, so there is a bit of a learning curve. Unfortunately all my games have been with inexperienced players, and there have been runaway leaders. There are some neat ideas here, but it does rely on other players to police each other, and it's not really my type of game. I do like the costs of playing cards and characters onto the board, but the rest of it just isn't my cup of tea. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
Although it looks like a dice-rolling wargame, battle is not the main thrust of the game. Playing cards effectively, selecting the right upgrades, and moving your units in response to a changing battlefield make for a lot of interesting strategy decisions. Lots of replay value since starting gods and board configuration is always different, and there is a large deck of event cards that affects play randomly.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A good heavy theme game.  You can still enjoy this game even if you are completely unfamiliar with the Warhammer world, however it will help to know it some.

I really enjoy the unbalanced powers.  They attempt to add up to the same amount of power, but just in different ways, and I like games that can do this.  Board map is a little confusing, but very artfully (new word) done.  There may be some weight issues, as far as one of the 4 factions being more powerful than the others, as well as a couple of misprints in the rules.

Overall, good game.

RATING: 10
"El Grande" with assymetrical conditions mates with Warhammer. 

The mechanisms aren't really revolutionary, but still, this is an exciting and demanding tactical game. Furthermore, the theme is striking like no other, very present and implemented in a marvellous way. The flavour text printed on the cards should always be read aloud for the sake of the atmosphere. The artwork is just awesome, although lacking functionality. 
What I like about the game play is that it has strong elements of "chaotic" player interaction with surprising outcomes. On the other hand, it is possible to plan ahead and make good decisions. The replay value seems to be high enough the moment I'm writing these lines.

It's one of the few FF games with roughly accurate game length denomination.

What a game! You are the ultimate evil bad guy! No...you cannot choose to play a good guy in this game! You are bad...the worst of the worst...and you have your own agenda for winning the game! And yet...this game is so simple to play!

RATING: 7.5
規則:
*放圖板每個腳色轉盤轉到開始處，毀壞卡照左上數字疊起1最上面，舊世界卡洗勻3/4人時使用8/7張，拿2貴族3傳送石4農民隨機放在九個地點
*隨機發腳色並照轉盤腳色順時鐘坐好(起始玩家為上方腳色)，每個玩家拿對應腳色的所有配件，分數標記放0，魔力標記放個人版標示處，每人洗勻自己的混亂卡並抽3張
*遊戲進行數輪，每輪有6個階段，達到結束條件就結束，有順序的部分依照圖板轉盤順序由上順時鐘結算
1.舊世界階段 開一張舊世界卡並執行效果，如果有選擇性由威脅值最低的選，如果有永久效果把之前放在效果去的左推放上去，只會有兩張有效果，被推出去的棄掉，有隕石邊的可能會因為一些效果被棄掉
2.抽牌階段 每人根據腳色圖板指示抽牌(有升級卡可多抽的)，抽完將棄牌洗勻繼續抽，無手牌上限，然後魔力回到標記處(有升級卡提升魔力)，之前沒用完的不會留
3.招喚階段 照順序輪流執行一個動作，直到pass時魔力移動到0，所有魔力到0的都直接跳過，全部人結束就結束
>招喚一個生物到場上，支付對應魔力，要跟之前放的在同一區或相鄰，移動視為重新招換要耗魔力(且一樣只能相鄰)
>出牌到地區執行效果並支付魔力，一個地區只能有兩張，左邊先結算
4.戰鬥階段 照圖板順序一個區域一個區域結算，所有場上的生物根據其戰鬥力擲對應數量骰，4以上算命中，6可再多丟一顆，照順位丟完後就決定要打誰在換下一位骰，不同玩家造成的傷害不累積，不能打友軍(被控制的算敵軍)，掛點的放倒但一樣會擲骰攻擊，農民一點就可以打掉拿走(提前戰鬥卡會先移除掛點的，且傷害可累積)
5.腐化階段 分成兩個主要階段
>計算征服值 每個區域照順序結算，每隻怪1點加上該區的卡牌魔力總合為該玩家征服值，該區最高的(平手就跳過)比較該區的抵抗值，如果超過則獲得該區分數(抵抗跟分數在該區下方數字，雖然區域初始一樣，可能被卡牌影響導致不同)
>腐敗 每個區域照順序每個信徒(1級兵)放一個腐敗標記，如果該區總和超過12(傳送石也算)則該去毀滅，抽最上面的毀滅卡放這，此回合有放腐敗標記的拿上面分數，毀滅卡不夠則區域不再毀滅
6.結束階段又分成6個階段結算
>移除場上所有混亂卡到各自棄牌堆
>結算有英雄標記的區域，該區有腳色且威脅值最高的玩家選擇一隻腳色回自己供應區
>左到右結算舊世界卡的回合效果
>毀滅卡記分，每個有該卡且開著的區域計算腐敗值，根據順位拿分數改卡蓋著，平手跟下個名次分數加起來平分(無條件捨去)
>每個轉盤上有威脅標記的轉一格，最多威脅標記得再轉一格(平手都不轉)，所有標記回供應區
*遊戲結束條件有四個，最後一個全輸，其他如果回合結束條件有達到就結束，第1個條件優先權最高，平手則比分數，其他都比分數，如果比分數時平手則比威脅值(轉盤值)
1.有人轉盤到達勝利格
2.有人分數到達50分
3.五個區域毀滅
4.舊世界卡抽完
**威脅輪盤 每個玩家獲得威脅標記的條件不同，獲得就放轉盤上，旋轉時升級就馬上獲得升級的好處，升兩級就拿兩次
**舊世界標記 事件標記舊根據卡片放置，都不移除，英雄標記結束階段結算，貴族標記增加地區的分數值，市民標記無效果可被收集，鼠人標記降低抵抗值，傳送石算腐敗值
*地區結算順序照地圖箭頭，且因為不是同時發生所以會互相影響
*被毀滅的地區 不能打卡，不能放標記，不會算征服分數，不會放腐敗標記
*如果卡牌效果打架，則取消效果優先
***腳色策略方針***
恐虐偏向打鬥跟轉盤勝利
納垢偏向分數勝利跟區域記分
奸奇勝利條件均分，偏向打卡，盡量先不耗魔力讓你獲得後期出卡優勢
色虐較彈性，著重貴族得分

*自動恐虐
行動順位 
3級怪在4地點 
2級怪在 5>6>3>7>8>2 毀滅後再 9>1>4>5>6>3
1級怪在4>5>6>3 毀滅後再 7>8>2>9>1
每輪照此表從上到下直到7魔力用完，先放供應區的在移動毀滅區的，如果有多餘魔力在倒序放2級怪
AI先行動，放置無視相鄰規則，不打卡片，如果要做決定由威脅值最低的做
攻擊目標 1級>3級>2級>農民，平手打威脅值高的
升級卡 增加一魔力>2 First Strike >3 Bloodsworn
放置貴族/傳送石/鼠人標記時照順序5>6>...
放置英雄/舊世界標記時1>9>2

RATING: 4
1 play - so I'm not an expert.

This game would have been better without dice (like Starcraft, Game of Thrones, Antike/Imperial, Tempus etc.). I don't see why they were included or how they improve the game. 

A potential problem might be that the winner is the player who gets picked on the least (or gets lucky), rather than the person who played best.  

I have heard/read comments about balance issues that require players to gang up on such-and-such a demon. 

It gets high ratings though so maybe I'm missing something.

RATING: 7
Perhaps surprisingly, the game this most reminds me of is A Game Of Thrones. You have build points. Use them to deploy units to areas on the board. Get VP's for an area if you've accumulated enough strength, and branch out from there. You have a unique condition to meet in as many areas as you can that progress you on your special power track, and if you progress enough along here you can win before the most VPs condition is met. Therefore, like aGoT, everyone has to carefully watch the other players and intervene if they're progressing too rapidly. There's some hit cards to play on other players which help, but the amount of whacking you can do each turn is limited as you only have so many build points. A random event each turn mixes things up (again, similar to aGoT).  The overall feel, once you're past all the theme and chrome, is simply a question of deployment each turn. You have 6 action points to progress both your special power action track AND accumulate VPs simultaneously, and it's a bonus if you can hinder opponents at the same time. The game will shine when all players are alert, but paradoxically the result will then be even more likely to then be a fallout of player interaction chaos. I enjoyed the ride the game gave and wouldn't say no to more, but I have a suspicion your success is more likely to be a result of other player's actions rather than your own.

RATING: 5
Non è proprio il mio tipo di gioco. Chaos in The Old World porta lo stampino della Fantasy Flight Games: manuale monumentale (32 pagine più eventuali errata... non è più un divertimento, è un lavoro), regole e regolette "di ambientazione" ("il giocatore ottiene una pedina squish per almeno ogni pedina squosh che piazza in fase di tracciamento in territori con almeno una pedina spruzz"), meccaniche pro-sega mentale e soprattutto, tema fantasy e grafica per bimbiminkia (cupo, artwork stucchevole e barocco, tutti cattivi, ecc.). La parte del tabellone con la mappa è un po' piatta (e i confini li ha disegnato uno più daltonico di me), e le rotelle sono belle a vedersi ma francamente gratuite. Mille carte e mille segnalini-sega riempiono mezzo tavolo con la scusa di "aggiungere varietà" al gioco.
Nonostante la mia bassa soglia di sopportazione per questo tipo di giochi, non mi sento di dargli un voto bassissimo perché, a differenza dei suoi predecessori FFG, almeno questo ha la decenza di avere una durata contenuta (alla fine, il 20% del tempo di gioco è fare il setup e rimettere a posto), e poi scorre più velocemente di quanto temessi (anche se mischiare meccaniche da eurogame e fuffa ameritrash è come bere un Sassicaia in un bicchiere di merda). Come al solito, i componenti sono di una qualità eccezionale.
Per quanto riguarda le fazioni, ho la sensazione che Khorne e Slaanesh siano molto più avvantaggiati nella progressione (gli basta un morto/una corruzione per avere un punto dominio, al contrario degli altri due che per farlo devono soddisfare due condizioni).
L'autore è uno dei responsabili del Briscolone Spaziale (Battlestar Galactica): benché si avverta la sensazione del "prendiamo un'idea e complichiamola all'infinito", a questo giro gli è venuta ugualmente male, ma un po' meno.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Initially the game can be overwhelming. You have 4 separate gods that all act differently and need to keep others in check. Meanwhile you're busy trying to throw your cultists into the mix to ruin regions. You want the most but then you become a target if you're leading in points.

I love how all this blends together into a nice meaty euro-y ameritrashy experience. Yes, the dice can completely fuck you over which is frustrating but with general planning and backup planning, I think that luck mostly mitigates itself. I have had games where I can't roll a hit to save my life and I've had games where my opponents roll nothing but 6's. Both are annoying but generally not completely unwinnable.

My biggest complaint about the game is the event deck which can be either completely random and useless or rather mean and vicious. I have issues with the map being functionally annoying as well based on the dark and gritty nature of the game. It can often be hard to see what's adjacent and what's actually a region.

Overall, I really enjoy the game, but it requires exactly 4 players (now 5 with the expansion) which has a TON of competition in the board gaming world.

RATING: 10
Fun Factor: 10 (The level of interaction here is just right.  A social game that also manages to be a thinker.  The perfect mix.)
Components: 10 (The good stuff.  Great models.  Good graphic design.  The board may be the weakest point, but I argue- it is precisely what is needed and no more.)
Theme: 10 (Amazing.  Playing the badguys just makes this game crackle with that much more electricity, because even the most stalwart goody-goody feels the call to backstab, outmaneuver and outwit his friends with impunity.)
Mechanics: 10 (Many different design ideas come together perfectly.)
Theme + Mechanics: 10 (Together the game is perfectly honed.  My favorite bit is how each player has two methods of victory and is working at them simultaneously. The cards give a perfect feel to each power involved.)
Replay Value: 9 (Every game has been different, and yet, there is a minute amount of similarity.  Very minute.)
Scaling/Players: N/A (This game needs four.  No less, no more.  Three can be attempted, but I feel the perfect balance is defeated.)

Subjective: +0.25 There are few games indeed that combine many disparate design elements, and in so doing, the whole of the pieces become transcendental.  This game has cards, alternate win conditions, area control, interaction and negotiation, variable player powers and secondary win conditions, an inbuilt timer... just to mention a few.  There is true genius here.

RATING: 9.42
Chaos deities.  An endless struggle.  Earth is a helpless wasteland, a stage for the monumental conflict between beings far more vast than humanity can understand.

So satisfying in its unwavering commitment to being completely over the f*cking top.  Everything is hideous and disgusting and beautiful.  

A lot of people are put off initially because of the seemingly huge battle advantage of Khorne.  This is before they realize this is not a battle game (if anything it is a card game!  With area control)  Once everyone knows how to play, it is the most gloriously balanced game I have ever witnessed.  The more everyone struggles, the more the balance is reinforced.  The harder they pull, the tighter the knot that binds everyone.  

The asymmetry is amazing.  Every player has their own set of cards which let them manipulate the game in any way imaginable, but each with their own personality.  The timing can be crushing, with feint after blow after undermining blow.  And in the end, when the dust settles and the ruination takes hold, someone manages to squeak ahead.  How?  Through sheer will and determination.  This game is a vehicle of ingenuity.  Enjoy the ride.

RATING: 10
My personal favourite at the moment. The undisputed champion. Demons fighting over supremacy of the Old Warhammer world. Killing, corrupting, magic, seduction, ever changing strategies, twists and turns make this a jewel of a game. The components are great, except for the cultists whose banners break easily. FFG sends replacements but they are just as fragile. You can score points by having control over territories, or invest in the future by corrupting them slowly for more points - just be sure your opponents don't get in front. If this does not suite you, just concentrate on fulfilling your unique godly interests and you will be rewarded with points, power upgrades and the chance to alter the map layout. Whoever gets to 50 points or completes his personal agenda first wins. But be sure to win before the world you fight over is destroyed or before the inhabitants of the world manage to find a way to send you back into the netherworld! Quite apocalyptic, if you ask me.

Finished painting all the figures.

RATING: 10
I have been playing games for a long time (since shortly after Moses descended the mountain). In all of that time, I have encountered only one other game that surprised me as Chaos in the Old World has. That game was Dune. In both of these games the mechanics are simple (place a few pieces, play a few cards) but the subtle and intricate complexities of the various encounters of these pieces and cards nearly baffles the mind. One or two plays of this game simply touches the surface. 

As with Dune, the time from the moment the game begins until it concludes is tense and fraught with critical decisions. The variety of paths each of the Chaos Lords may take to victory forces each player to not only attempt scoring for themselves but prevent opponents from scoring. Layered on this is the possibility that preventing one opponent from scoring may be just what is required for a different opponent to score.

After just a few plays I would have to say that this is the best game Lang has designed and possibly the best game Fantasy Flight publishes. The game play, the art, the components...it is sheer excellence.

*****
After many, many plays, this game never ceases to amaze me. Something unexpected has occurred each time we play and the tension each turn continues. As with Dune, this game can be blunt or very subtle.

RATING: 5
Thematic game where as a demon, you try to ruin the world in the way you want it ruined as opposed to the way your opponents want to ruin it. This is done through playing cards in one of two zone slots and by having your minis fight other minis. Each turn the player who did their thing the best gets two "twists" of their dial which facilitate upgrades or in some cases total victory. This can get a little exhausting.

:star: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:nostar: Handles 5 or more players
:nostar: Good with different player counts
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:nostar: Re-playability
:star::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment

RATING: 5

RATING: 6.75
CITOW is definitely a FFG game. You can tell by the mechanics, the bits and the theme. But also because it is a mixture of other FFG games, effectively it borrows a lot from Middle Earth Quest, BSG and War of the Ring. Anyhow, it is a good game and all the Ameritrash laurels are in there, various player powers, flavouring, global effects, specific effects, dice and so forth. I never played Warhammer before but I feel it emulates the gritty world quite well. The strategies are varied and I enjoyed how the outcome could not be predicted by the veteran player. Still, I would have been more impressed by this if it was not for many similar games by FFG I have player before.

RATING: 7.5
The base game has issues, but shows promise.
Couple this together with The Horned Rat however, and this rivals Game of Thrones.

My first few times playing I often felt like the map was claustrophobic, and there weren't enough actions to implement a meaningful plan.  But each time I would come away ruminating along the lines of "Well, if I had done X then maybe..."  or "Oh now I get it, this is meant to be used like so..."  

The strategy runs deep and despite not having the grandest time, this game always leaves me wanting to try it again with new tactics.

RATING: 8
My thoughts on the game are as chaotic as those awful chaos cards...by midway through my first game I was thinking this would be another AT once-off. Too many "take that!" powerful chaos cards, frustrating inability to plan ahead and lots of whining of the "why did you play that on me, I'm coming last" variety. 

But things improved dramatically (and the game finished in a similar manner) towards the end. You can plan against some of the cards, combat works nicely, the asymmetric powers mean a lot of repeat plays. Very cool AT design (just because it scores with area control doesn't mean it is a euro - this is 100% AT). 

Don't really like the theme. The chaos mythology always gave me the creeps. The gods remind me of images people come up with after a really bad drug trip. But still, it works, it's quick, there's a lot to think about. Could be an AT game of choice.

RATING: 10
I must admit this one is one of the best games i ever played!
I am not a huge fan of warhammer theme but i found out that every god is perfectly made with their skills and strategies to win.

Every game is decided in the last round, comebacks are easily made. The mot important things is that luck does not play a crucial role for the final victory! I have seen people winning even with awful dice rolls (Even if you play as Khorne).

Even if the old world cards plays against you, you can always play to end the game in a draw by playing totally against the others.

You have to play in a group when all 4 players are equally skilled and hopefully with a lot of experience in this game. Then you will have a lot of fun!

RATING: 8
Lots to like here. Vivid (if unsavoury) theme; fairly straightforward mechanics, red-blooded conflict; and it's playable in about two hours. Best of all, the factions are fundamentally different in their approaches and victory conditions. Lots of wary tracking of victory status, followed by sudden assaults on rivals. Game has a satisfying dramatic arc, with tentative placements followed by raging conflict followed by desperate endgame.

However, I have to knock a point or two for the piss-poor graphic design. The map is appallingly disfunctional.

RATING: 8.1
Though this game has a lot of Games Workshop theme it is essentially a multiplayer strategic wargame in which players try to acquire a target number of points. There are essentially two different tracks of points, though I am not yet clear if one is needed as the game seems to be consistently won by one method rather than the other.

Each player controls a particular Chaos power, the powers have their own unit types, and sets of cards. They take turns to deploy troops, or cards, to zones, which is followed by battles, and attempts to dominate regions.

Nothing here is really new but what sets the game a little apart is that it seems to play very well with three players. The tactical dynamics tend to prevent a kick the leader effect. So while I probably wouldn't bother with this as a four player game, there are things I would rather have a go at, its certainly worth digging out when you have three players at the table.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I played this for the first time at the start of January 2015, with gamers I don't game with often - and for this reason I don't expect to play again for a long time, perhaps not ever. It was a 5-player game, with the Horned Rat expansion - and it was lots of fun. Bear in mind that I tend to prefer Eurogames and dislike most games from Fantasy Flight - but I don't object to a little bit of dice-based combat, and everything else in this game was right up my street. Variable player powers, mainly manifested in a different deck of cards for each player, lots of tough decisions and tension. I'd love to play this again, and would even like to own it, although I know my wife really wouldn't enjoy it so it's not a priority for me.

RATING: 6.9
(+) Very thematic game
(+) The four fractions all play very differently, but still the game is very balanced: I have won games with all fractions and in my experience all games ended in a very exciting close race to victory for most players
(+) Different style of each fraction increases the replay-value
(+) The chaos cards are evil - in a good way :)

(-) Complex rules. Explaining the game takes quite a while.
(-) I enjoy playing all fractions except for Khorne: I don't like the interaction of killing off other player's units and I certainly don't like dice. Still, the Khorne player is of the utmost importance for the dynamic of the game
(-) To enjoy a balanced game of Chaos in the old world, it is important for each fraction to stay strategically focused. If one fraction miserably falters, the game might become unbalanced.
(-) Some cards in the old world deck are unbalanced and unfair to certain fractions. I'm not so much concerned with luck-dependence due to dice rolls, but I am very much concerned with luck-dependence due to crazy old world cards! The deck also always seems to favor one faction (e.g. if lots of heroes and nobles come out early, this will aid Slaanesh. If lots of warpstones come out, this will be an important benefit to Tzeentch, if the cards increase competition in populous areas, that will be a disadvantage to Nurgle, ...). This might bring a problematic imbalance to the game.

RATING: 9
16-07-2014. Rated after 1 play. Admission: I couldn't care less about Warhammer and its lore. But this an excellent game, in which you play chaos lords trying to destroy humanity/corrupt the world (or something). It's basically an area control game with some lasting influence in regions that can get you VPs later. 

Each round, an old world card is flipped to represent some event in the world. Then, players take turns spending resources to play cards, put down/move figures.

After everyone runs out of resources, battles are resolved in the battle phase. If there are multiple figures in an area belonging to different chaos lords, they will fight. Fights are very easy, you just roll the dice allowed by your army, and for every roll of 4+ you deal one hit to a unit.

Then, if you have more figures + card value in an area than or other players and exceed that areas resistance (which is determined by a printed value on the map and the influences of several old world cards) you score VPs equal to the conquest value (printed value on the board). 

Then, the game continues to the corruption step. Each cultist, one type of figure in the board will add 1 corruption marker to the board. Subsequently, areas are scored when there are 12 corruption markers (tokens of cultists presence in the region). Only the first and second most corruptive influence score, and in addition the players that added tokens this round also score an additional couple of points. 

Besides these ways to get VPs, you can get additional VPs by achieving chaos lord specific goals (through either meeting specific conditions and earning tick advancements) or by using cards/ achieving dominance over regions. 

The game ends in several ways.. and it's possible to all lose. Interestingly not necessarily the one with the most VPs will win. A more important way to win the game is by achieving dial advancements, these ticks are earned by fulfilling chaos lord specific goals and you need 8-9 of those to win the game. This winning condition is checked first, so you could in theory win with 20 VPs or so, but with those advancements before someone with 50 Vps would win. If the chaos deck runs out, everybody loses.

This game is very cool. Each chaos lord has different ways to win, different unit characterists and different strengths/weaknesses. These apparently are derived from warhammer lore and make much sense. 

We played the basegame with 4 new players in about 2.5 hours, including rule searches. We'd probably will be able to bring playtime down to about 1.5-2hours depending on experience of players.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5

RATING: 7
Someone said to me that he thought in COW he had maybe finally found a (bit of heavier) eurogame that he liked. That totally caught me off guard. I had never even considered that this game would be called a euro. Recently I've been playing a bunch of eurogames and COW feels completely differently to me. For me COW feels like a conquest/conflict game with some subtle euro elements woven in. But the more I think about it the more it does seem like a hybrid, leaving open how you see it, totally depending on your perspective. If you almost exclusively play American style games, COW will probably seem more like a euro to you.

I've played two games so far, both of them with three players. Played as Slaanesh first and won what was essentially a draw. The second game played with Nurgle showed to me the depth of strategy possible with this game because I really did not play Nurgle well at all. But after I thought about what I could have done. I like that, a game allowing/leading you to reflect on how the game played out. Khorn had run away with the game, getting pretty lucky with the Old World cards giving him almost unlimited peasants to kill and have 4-7 clicks every round. Of course, that's the wrong way to play, which we realized days after playing (Khorn only gets a click after killing figures from another player).

Now I've played a third game with five players (including the expansion). Liked it just as much, still lots to think about and strategy to work through and build on. Even though I did pretty well (far behind the leader but in second place) it felt maybe a bit crowded on the board with five players. I'm wondering if 4 players is the perfect number. But in two of the thre games the Old World cards (affecting the overall world play for a round or more) seemed to me to affect the game too much. It's not really just that some individual cards affect things too much, it's that there often seems to be a series of cards that together in a row can affect the game so much that they alone decide the outcome. Especially when one Old World card stays in play for  over three rounds and has very damaging results for certain players. It is nice that they serve to create some extra variety from game to game though.

I really like this game, even though the only thing I know of the Warhammer world I learned from playing this game. That's testament to a well designed strongly thematic game.

RATING: 8.25
This area control game was the first one I played in which you and your opponents can occupy the same terrain on the board without immediately having this situation resolved through combat, which I still think is a great mechanic.  I haven't yet played another area control game with the card-slotting mechanic that Chaos uses, which seems crazy, because that is another brilliant mechanic for this sort of game.  Asymmetrical player factions/gods gives the game further points in my book, especially that each faction has a unique win conditions (at least, unique criteria to advance towards victory) and I love that there are multiple ways to win, in general, with the victory points and the victory dial.

I'd recommend this to anyone who likes area control games, and even say that those who usually don't like conflict should consider giving this a shot.  Some factions in particular aren't looking to go out and smash the opposition with the cutthroat attitude which a player often needs in this kind of game, but rather are effective when sitting back and scheming to gradually develop control of an advantageous position.

RATING: 8
Visual Appeal: (4) 
Very nice looking game with great art and nice board layout with the power dials. I wish the board used more contrasting colors that just black and brown. Sometimes it's a strain to find things.

Components: (4) 
Would be a 5 except for the cultist pieces. They are so small that the fragile staff/sigil of each piece bends and breaks with normal use and just looks shoddy. The rotating upgrade dials are one of my favorite components in any game because they are just fun and intuitive. Cards are high quality standard of FFG.

Theme: (5)
The theme was one of the first things that grabbed me about this game. Who doesn't want to be a crazy all-powerful being who wreaks havoc upon puny towns and cities? The game mechanisms use the theme well since all of the player abilities are asymmetrical and specific to their character. Corruption placement and domination are standard area control fare with a demonic flair.

Learning: (3) 
The rules have some errors. Easily fixable but something that shouldn't happen with proper editing. To really learn the game requires knowing the powers all 4 players' cards and dials so that you can work to counteract them. I've made my own player cheat sheets for the game to help everyone remember. This knowledge comes with experience, but can be a big hurdle for new players.

Gameplay: (4)
There is a lot of "screw you" in this game. I feel like the asymmetry is well balanced when players know the game well. Certain plays can create devastating advantage against those players who don't know how to counter them like Tzeench's "Warp Shield" or Nurgle's "Final Rotting." I think Slaanesh won by dial advancement in the first 3 games because while learning, we couldn't figure out how to counter that strategy fairly. Definitely a game that changes over time when you play with the same group. That's good because you are often rewarded for trying new angles.

Overall Fun: (4)
This is a great game with good replay value. I enjoy it, but getting exactly 4 players together who want to play has proven difficult. Everyone with whom I've played counts it among their favorites.

RATING: 9.5
I love this game. Its design is just so masterful, and it is dripping with theme. It combines so many of the elements that I love from all three "families" of board games. 

Set in the Warhammer 40K universe, this is Ameritrash gaming at its best, with a deep and engaging theme and plenty of dice-rolling combat. But it's essentially an area control eurogame, complete with victory point track. At the same time, it kind of "feels" like a wargame.

You don't have to win by victory points. There are other paths to victory. And the four factions play asymmetrically and yet appear to be perfectly balanced.

I was just amazed that the designer was able to create such a beautiful and complex design without having it fall apart. It is seamless. It is superb. It feels epic, and yet it can play in a couple of hours.

I sincerely hope I get to play this again soon.
