RATING: N/A
Bought from Guild of Blades retail on 9/13/2016. $59.95. Out of stock everywhere online due to Fantasy Flight split with Gamesworkshop announcement.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Really an area-majority Euro at heart, but the asymmetric powers and cards are so well done that the cubes truly morph into unique figures. Each faction has a point of differentiation in every single facet of the game. Puts all those Euros that half-assedly differentiate each side (really, Age of Empires? Just 1-2 national advantages to call yourself differentiated? that's it?) to shame.

And the Euro mechanisms are completely integrated into the game, unlike Cyclades, where the auction sticks out like a sore thumb. Chaos doesn't introduce anything that's dramatically new, but glues together its thoughtfully implemented parts seamlessly. A true Euro-Ameritrash hybrid. The cards, variable powers and lurid theme scream Ameritrash, but excellently integrated European design principles include:

 - if you fall behind early, cards will favor you. 
 - the action budgeting system (a la Through the Ages and La Strada) results in virtually no downtime. 
 - a gazillion ways to score VPs, but counteracted by a brilliant balanced-scoring concept. There are two often-divergent ways to win: -- dials or VPs -- and you need to equally go down both paths, before deciding which objective to push for.

Rules were really easy to learn, especially with the player aid, and a 4-player game took only 2 hours!

I'm convinced this can be re-themed into a pooping game (the corruption tokens) to show the more prejudiced of Eurogamers what they're missing out on! Or just give it the El Grande Sterilization Treatment.

Need to see how the balance is, but currently it's a 10 from me.

5+5

g{[b]Favorite 4-player Med-length[/b]}g
g{[b]Favorite 5-player Med-length[/b]}g (with Horned Rat expansion)

RATING: 10
An absolute rarity. (I would say a diamant, but diamants aren't rare, just overpriced). It's a rare case of FFG game that's finalized and balanced right off the bat. No expansion needed to fix it. (Expansion is basically complete re-imagening). It's a rare case of a nonbloated ameritrash and FFG design. Of course FFG couldn't help themselves, so the board is illegible. Because: atmospheresomethingsomethingchildishsupposedlydarkhumoursomething.
 
But. It is brilliant. Like a diamant. An asymmetrically cut one, because, well, that's the shtick. It's a DoaM/Area majority AT/Euro hybrid. With asymmetrical sides. Which are balanced. Uhm. Yes, they are. Shuttup, they are. Look. Citow is a gem game and as such is to be properly appreciated under very specific conditions. You need 4 players. Citow does not scale. And the way Citow is balanced is the way players are supposed to balance it. And the way players are going to learn how to balance it is for the same group of players (which consists as we've established of exactly 4 persons) are going to play it time and time and time again. And then some. So if you say this gem game isn't balanced, it's your fault for not training yourself in balance achieving skills. 

I've stuck to my copy which wasn't getting any play, till miraculously a group appeared of 4 dudes (I'll count myself as a dude), and we played it over and over and over again. Pure bliss. (also because no rules explanation). And it is balanced. And games are tense when everybody knows what they should be doing. Because playing Citow is like enacting a theatre play - these Gods are entangled in eternal dance in which everybody has their role and their job in balancing the cosmic scale. Those who say "game plays you" "no freedom" and blahblahyadayada should raise their gaze from the hand of cards and stop looking for options there - your options are the other players. 

[And oh yeah, my #3 game.]
[And another oh yeah, this fixes all my problems with El Grande (theme disconnect, adjacency) so no reason to play that one anymore.]

RATING: 10
One of my first, and still my favourite game ever. It offers a perfect combination of "take that" elements, with clever euro mechanics and ameritrash combat. RATING: 9
[i]Fantastic and Convincing Attempt at Truly Asymmetric Play in a Multiplayer Strategy Game![/i]

Best with 4 players.

Note: Base game is a great framework and an awesome standalone game on its own, but the expansion, which changes the game quite a bit (replacing every card in the game) I found to be more my style, so I maintain a different rating for that. I'm also more experienced with the expansion.

Love the genuine asymmetry, and the main thing this game has going for it is the pacing and playtime compared to other similar games. Lots of game crammed into 90 minutes means that every single turn will be exciting.

Lots of good stuff to say. It's not an epic area control game like Dominant Species, but the overwhelmingly awesome gameplay/playtime ratio makes me pretty much always eager to play.

The Horned Rat Expansion bumps the rating up to "10" for me  because of the more interesting cards. Things I think the expansion does better:

* The chaos cards in the expansion are more interesting. I think there are less useless ones, and most of them promote interaction between players. However, I think the cards are better themed in the base game, which I miss, especially for Nurgle.

* Negates the importance of the dial victory for Khorne in particular. While this may sound like a negative, dial victories are less interesting than VP ones usually, as missing a double dial for a couple turns can doom you. All gods should be able to win by points.

* Expansion has upgrades that are actually interesting and situational enough to not get the same picks every single game. Still not perfect, as there are some clearly underpowered ones, but better I think.

* 5 players, more flexibility, and seems to play better with 3.

* Warriors and Daemons that aren't a waste of money for everyone but Khorne and Nurgle.

Both games are great though.

Base Game: (5 Design + 4 Enjoyment)
With Expansion: (5 Design + 5 Enjoyment)

RATING: 6.5
Nice little asymmetrical conflict driven area-control game.  It's El Grande where you get to punch the leader in the face instead of the gut.  Lots of thought went into making every faction feel individual.  I question whether this actually limits the replayability as factions have a distinct path to victory.  There is an illusion of multiple paths to victory, and it seems that it is just that there are specifically different paths that only some of the factions can achieve.

Not being aware of each of the faction's strategies will lead to a disappointing game.  A play under the belt or some quick tips for faction goals should lead to a more balanced game.  It seems that each player is designed to keep the player to their left in check, so if one player isn't doing so hot, their counterpart will thrive and come into a clean victory.  We'll see.  It certainly expects players to fall on their sword to stop others from gaining ground.  I fear kingmaker situations.

The sculpts are nice (cultists are fragile), though strangely not in correspondence to the world that they are trying to emulate (wrong # of breasts and eyes for certain creatures).  Not a biggie, though.  The board is a bit tough to read, so much noise.  You get used to it, kind of like looking at a magic eye (autostereogram); your mind cleans up the jumbled mess.

A fine enough, multiplayer bash the leader euro-based conflict game if you're looking for that kind of thing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is an area control game that plays very differently from the typical "I have more units in this region than you" area control fare.  In this game each player controls a different malevolent god that has their own agenda of corruption and destruction.  This is accomplished by either summoning units in different areas or playing "Chaos Cards" into those areas.  The cards provide special bonuses to the player but also adds its cost to the domination total for a region that scores that player points if he has the most at the end of any given round.  The basic figure for each player (the cultists) also corrupt the land and enough corruption also provides players points.

Each character also plays very differently, like I said they all have their own agenda and accomplishing their agenda provides them with "dial advancements" that provide players with bonuses and also getting enough is one of the game's victory conditions.  I think it is rare, however, that the game ends any other way than a player achieving 50 victory points.

I think the way the game plays is very interesting.  There are battles but there is only ever one battle in any area in a given round and anybody left in the area after the skirmish gets to cohabitate for another round.  Corruption is placed by all players cultists in that area so they even get to work together to ruin the land.  I think I'm most impressed at how very different all the characters are yet none of them seem to have an immediately clear advantage.

However, despite how varied and different the characters are and how much I love asynchronous player powers and abilities, there wasn't much variety in the game.  Compare it to something like Kemet, each player starts out the same in that game but through what powers they claim creates a very different profile throughout.  Each game you play of Kemet you can try (and even be forced to) a different strategy and each game feels much more varied and new than this game.  You can try each of the 4 different characters but that provides you with 4 games that each feel new.  After that every time you play the same character is going to feel exactly the same.  You don't really have room to try new strategies because each character needs to focus on one thing or you are just going to do poorly.  Even the deck of Chaos Cards each character has seems to actually be just multiple copies of the same 5 or 6 cards.

The other problem I have with this game, and it is just a personal preference thing, is the game is a bit too gruesome for me.  Don't get me wrong, the art and figures are fantastic and detailed, and that's the problem.  I don't necessarily want to play a game too often where I am staring at a map drawn on stretched human skin or a detailed miniature of a demon tearing his own stomach open and letting the contents spill to the floor.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
When played with four players familiar with each faction, this is my favorite area control game.  Playing with a skilled group of players will constantly force you to adapt your strategy.  Decision trees that you may have made min-max evaluations against will constantly be discarded as each phase progresses.  

Asymmetric victory paths, politicking, free-for-all conflict, and kingmaker scenarios may discourage gamers who favor cooperative games or strategy games with little interactivity.  The number of combinations of Old World cards, player hands, and Old World tokens makes it likely each game state will be unique.

This game is very well balanced for 4 players.  A 3-player game should only be attempted with players who have many 4-player game sessions under their belts.  Players who are experts with each faction can make 3-players games fun and challenging as well.

RATING: 8
Perhaps more than any other game I've read about on this site, "Chaos in the Old World" has received almost universally effusive word-of-mouth praise.  And that's why I bought it.  And I'm glad I did!  For a game that combines many different mechanisms (area control, conquest, card play, resource management, special player powers, etc.), it is relatively simple to learn and plays quite quickly.  As for the "chaos", the game is a little chaotic, but not to the point where players feel like they are not in control of what is happening.  And as for the "old world", I have absolutely no previous experience with the world of Warhammer, and normally my personal preference leans toward fun and light themes rather than darker ones, but let's be honest, there's something quite gratifying about playing an evil god who is trying its best to corrupt the world!  And all those plastic critters are fantastic!!  If I had hours and hours of spare time, I'd definitely paint all of them.  So what are the negatives?  Well, in my limited experience so far, it seems that players must spend too much time preventing the Korne player from obtaining dial advancement tokens, which takes away from pursuing their own strategies.  And the end game tends to come rather suddenly, leaving a slightly anticlimactic feeling.  Oh, and there's the fact that you really need to play with exactly 4 players.  But overall, this is a fantastic game which successfully bridges the gap between Ameritrash and Euro-style games.  Game design: 4/5  Personal enjoyment: 4/5
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The only game that has ever made me think, "there's too much theme in my euro". It's hard to see the game for all the grungy Warhammer bling.

What finally made me decide to trade the game away was the threat dials and what they represent. By way of context, each faction has a threat track of different length (longest for Khorne, shortest for Slaanesh) and a different condition for advancing on it; one of the win conditions is advancing to the end. In a eurogame, this would be handled with an elegant table of some kind: you can see at a glance who is about to win, what bonuses they'll get for advancing and so on. Here it's mystery meat: a "threat dial" which rotates, showing the next bonus through its cut-out window.

Experienced players know the details, so it's not hidden information. It's just a "feature" bolted on because someone at Fantasy Flight had a clever idea in the shower that morning. And that's Chaos in the Old World in a nutshell: it plays surprisingly well, but elegant it ain't -- it feels like a bunch of different ideas thrown into a blender.

RATING: 9
I'm just trying to think if there has been any other game I've played where I had as strong a feeling of "Wow, why hasn't a game like THIS been designed before?"

Plays in 2 hours, 4 very different roles, a bit of dice, great theme, very close which is assisted by the dual victory conditions so that at least 2-3 people should be in the hunt at the end.  Potential kingmaking is usually mitigated by the fact that all placement occurs before all combat.  The card decks are terrific (a few cards are mispriced perhaps, but whatever...), and the Old World cards keep things from getting too scripted.

Excellent game.  I'm looking forward to new games like this, as I think this basic template has a lot of promise.

RATING: 8

Good fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I like it how plays, in theory, and it can give an enjoyable experience, but I find a lot of flaws in it. Many of the upgrade cards will never see use, while others will always be first choices. Ditto with many chaos cards.
People praise the games asymmetry, but 3 of the 4 powers just aren't all that different and are essentially competing in an area majority game. The fourth power is playing a completely different game, which throws an (undesirable, IMHO) wrench into things. Because Khorne only needs to kill to advance, and gains a huge power boost after 2 advances, the other powers must all work on blunting Khorne turn 1. I really don't like how scripted this makes the first turn. I feel like you can't try anything new, because if it doesn't work you hand the game to Khorne.
Khorne also blocks off one of the victory routes. Because Khorne can score more dial advancement tokens than any other power, no one else can realistically go for a dial win unless Khorne allows them to.
All put together, this is a game that just doesn't work well. Combat is a sadly minor and usually self-destructive part of a game that should feature it.
I'm aghast that people talk about how well balanced it is; its almost as badly balanced as a game where people can gang up on the leader can possibly be.
If you want a similar game that's a lot better designed, try El Grande. Its also more bloodthirsty, which is a sad statement.
And now CitOW has been completely blown away by Cthulhu Wars, which does a similar theme and asymmetric player powers much betters.

RATING: N/A
Very enjoyable, fresh and climatic. An impressive board and fairly good figures (though some of them have fragile banners). Really well designed and rather balanced powers of four players (Chaos Gods), unique victory conditions. A very meaty game.

There is a significant random element involved: the appearing events might change your chances drastically. Also, the king-making factor is not negligible: if your opponents are fighting between themselves, you have higher chances to win. The game is the best with a group of four experienced players keeping eyes on others. If you play in a mixed team (experienced, un-experienced), the game may be decided by a un-wise moves of a newbie.   

Number of plays: 21.

RATING: 8.5
Rated this upwards over time. This is a game in which the faction you'll be playing contributes a LOT to whether you'll enjoy this or not.

For me, I took Tzeentch the first to times, as scheming and careful 'behind the scenes' play is right up my ally. However, as with Tzeentch it is impossible to win if others are not veterans (so someone will not play their part and allow Nurgle or Slaanesh to grab VP or dial-victory respectively), that detrimented a lot to me enjoying the game.

Enter the Horned Rat and a great, contested, 5p game during which 3 of the 5 players made a shot for the win (Khorne, Nurgle and myself), only winning on Dial victory the turn Nurgle hit 52 points. Great game, if Slaanesh hadn't maneuvered himself into a corner which he never actually got out we would all be taking a shot at the win. 

Great asymmetric game which will compete vs A Game of Thrones for our 'light wargaming' gamespot. Especially recommended for people that dig the Games Workshop universe!

RATING: 5

A couple of things ding some points for me though.  One, I'm not sure how each of the gods are balanced. This is idle speculation at this time though.

As far as the graphic design.  This game is probably a poster child for how NOT to illustrate a board game.  Although everything looks nice.  The board is a chore to play on, and it's a constant struggle to see what's going on in certain areas.  When I'm trying to internalize rules and formulate a plan, I don't want to have to look up and then figure out what the current situation is, I want to look at the board and know.

RATING: 7.5
Good fun! Mechanically, this is pretty strong - it handles territory control and asymmetric roles very well. Each Chaos God requires a different play style. Often this results in some imbalances as it is easy for the other players to allow Khorne to win and your group needs to have worked out some of this stuff.

Again, sticking with the mechanics, the points of criticism I'd mention would be:
- there is a lot of public information - trying to remember the combined effects of a whole bunch of special powers, cards, and world effects is a pain.
- the "dials" are really a pretty obscure way to present the progress of the Gods towards world domination.

All of this makes perfect sense given the theme. As it happens, I love the fact you're playing a bunch of evil Chaos gods - if you don't like that concept you won't like the game - and all the asymmetry, bizarreness and "take-that"-ness builds towards it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Player specific abilities, area control, dice, deeply integrated theme ... this is not a Euro. Typically I hate games like this (hate is a bit strong, but they're typically not my favorite games). Somehow, this one blew me away after the first play. First, there's no player elimination (terribly common in games like this). In fact, players continue to have an impact regardless how far behind they might fall. I have come from behind with significant setbacks on this game, so I know it can be done. Second, the player specific abilities are intersting enough that even where there might be imbalance the game becomes engaging to try and figure out how best to play that specific chaos power. To me this translates as at least 4 engaging, discovery oriented plays (already good in my book). Third, the area control element has layers which allow multiple avenues for domination. This allows for deep tactical play while following an over-arching strategy for your chaos power. Finally, the battles are quick and not too painful. I hate dice. I don't like them any better in this game, but at least it doesn't involve long, dragged out sequences of adding up bonuses and rerolling a thousand random dice to determine some pitiless god's will regarding the game's result.

I highly recommend this game to both sides of the fence for heavy gamers (Euro and Thematic) as I suspect both will find it fun and engaging while also having enough solid mechanics to scratch that mathematical itch so many of us have.

RATING: 8
This game might actually deserve a higher rating - the thing is, this usually isn't my type of game at all, but this game just does it so beautifully that I still enjoy it. The fact that each race is completely different and plays completely differently is just so much fun. each race feels an important part of the balance in this game - Some races focuses on themselves, creating the optimal path to Victory, and if you leave them alone they will beat you by miles. While others have great harassment possiblities and it is their job to make sure everyone receives equal punishment otherwise someone is going to run off with the Victory.

That is the beauty of this - I usually don't like games where you have to attack and harass and ruin someone's day - but in this game you have to!  One races litterally gets its points this way and you know if they don't, they don't stand a chance. It's much more thematic and logical reasoning then in most war and combat games where it's just "And now I attack you cause I don't like your face". The only minor issue with this is that sometimes it feels that the Winner is decided more by the balance of Things between players and not so much by your brilliant secret startegy.

RATING: 5

This is outside of my normal realm of enjoyment, but I can admire the design and how differently feeling and balanced the four factions were created.  My first game had the winner decided because of the order the territories were checked for ruination.  It was a disappointment to have a game come down to that situation.  I'm worried that with competent skilled play, the winner will just be decided by Khorne in an accidental kingmaker sort of way.  I am willing to play this occasionally if someone is waxing for it, and I was surprised I didn't hate it.  I rather enjoy the cultists being weak in battle, but are required to score points.  It does a lot of things really right, and a lot of things I do not care for.  Expertly crafted game that falls just outside of my tastes.

Note:  I am a eurogamer through and through and I prefer this game to El Grande.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
If you like games like Dune, this is a game for you!
The production value is very high. The rules are simple and well explained. The mechanisms are intuitive and well put together. There is balance by all powers to be unbalanced! Great stuff from FFG. All 4 powers have their own agenda. It is not a war game, but a game of advancing your agenda faster than other players. You: the player , define your agenda by the way. Each power has special abilities but you need to define how to win. Very clever game. Most games are won by victory points, and not so much via Threat dial, but the Threat dial makes all the difference in the world to win. Very tight game. First time you will play it, you will loose badly.

The rule book is well written and with many examples. Then they made this typo in the player's aids and everyone complaints about it (and rightly so). The Slaanesh advances its dial when 2 (and not 1) or more corruption tokens are placed in a region with a Noble or a Hero. The Khorne advances its dial when you kill 1 or more figures (but not peasants) in the same region and phase.

RATING: 7
Really great dudes on a map game.  The play of the spells is cool, but some spells are useless which upsets me.  

What makes this game wonderful is the interplay of the powers.  This is what you should aim for in games with asymmetric powers.  Each player has their role of who and where and when they need to be using their specific strengths to counter each opponents specific weaknesses.  Everyone must keep everyone in check.  The player who can trick the others into not being kept in check, even for just a moment, will be the winner.  

The dice are fine.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Best of my Essen 2009 purchases sofar. I am not a big fan of Fantasy Flight, feeling that half their games (especially the big boxes) are unnecessarily complex, have a lot of unnecessary components and too many rules that do not matter too much. Luckily, none of these arguments holds against Chaos In The Old World. It feels like a streamlined majority game with multiple victory tracks. It has a sense of urgency that persists throughout the game. It has little downtime and a very reasonable playing time. It is highly interactive without becoming chaotic. It has a great theme that might not appeal to everyone, but will certainly please the crowd that grew up with Warhammer. I loved it with three (upon which this rating is based) and expect to like it even better with four. Great game.
UPDATE: Played the game a couple of times with four and liked it even better that way. Every game was full of suspense and equally liked by all players. Have no choice but to raise my rating to a 9 - only substracting a point for two reasons: 1) the game really is four player only (limited flexibility) and 2) upgrade card selections feel obvious (making the game feel a little bit linear).

RATING: 7
It's area majority, but with lots of chrome and battle thrown on.  I really enjoy the variable player powers, which along with the event cards provide a high replayability.  All four factions play quite differently.  The components, of course, are up to Fantasy Flight's usual high quality, and the theme is pretty neat, even if I know nothing of the Warhammer world.  The idea of being evil gods corrupting stuff is just cool.  There are a couple drawbacks.  The Old World event cards can swing the game a bit too much,  and Khorne is strong enough that the rest of the players usually have to gang up to take him down, though the expansion mitigates this to a certain degree.  This is worth playing for both Eurogamers and Ameritrashers, as there's plenty here for either to like.  Really a tight design.

RATING: 5
Initially, I was put off on this game because it had an area control mechanism, but the variable powers of the races, combined with the various different ways to win the game, mean that the game doesn't have to be played strictly off of that one mechanism.  One can't ignore that aspect of the game by any means, but it's not necessary to maintain control over the regions in order to win the game.  It does require a familiarity with all the gods and their abilities to play it well, so it's hard to judge off of just one play, but it seems to be a nice alternative to strict area control games.  At the very least, I like the mechanism where the players have a certain amount of energy to use each turn, and how it affects the order in which you play your cards in a turn.  It's a neat tension that may not be unique, but works well in the game.

1 play: 4 players

RATING: N/A
Chaos in the Old World makes you a god. Each god’s distinctive powers and legion of followers grant you unique strengths and diabolical abilities with which to corrupt and enslave the Old World.
\13\
  * Khorne, the Blood God, the Skulltaker, lusts for death and battle.\13\
  * Nurgle, the Plaguelord, the Father of Corruption, luxuriates in filth and disease.\13\
  * Tzeentch, the Changer of Ways, the Great Conspirator, plots the fate of the universe.\13\
  * Slaanesh, the Prince of Pleasure and Pain, the Lord of Temptations, lures even the most steadfast to his six deadly seductions.
\13\\13\
Yet, as you and your fellow powers of Chaos seek domination by corruption and conquest, you must vie not only against each other, but also against the desperate denizens of the Old World who fight to banish you back to the maelstrom of the Realm of Chaos.
\13\\13\
Chaos in the Old World features three ways to win, and gives you an unparalleled opportunity to reshape the world in your image. Every turn you corrupt the landscape, dominating its inhabitants, and battle with the depraved followers of rival gods. Each god has a unique deck of gifts and abilities, and can upgrade their followers into deadly foes. Summon forth living manifestations of Chaos, debased and hidden cultists, and the horrifying greater daemons - beings capable of destroying near everything in their path.

RATING: 7
A very impressive game design that features four completely asymetric sides. This would seem incredibly hard to pull off, but Chaos in the Old World's game play is smooth as silk and the balance is razor sharp. It's a little weird to see the dice happy Warhammer universe in a Euro game, but the theme and god powers are so well done that it doesn't take long to completely forget the oddity of it all.

I've only been able to play it once, so I am going to rate the game on my first impression. Hopefully I will get to play a few more times, because I have a strong feeling this rating is only going to go up as I get more comfortable with the game and its interactions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[i]Rating with the Horned Rat expansion added to the base game (8/10 without the expansion).[/i]

I didn't fully appreciate this game after first couple of plays. I thought it was very good, but certainly not amazing. Well, the thing is that you need the Horned Rat expansion to experience the game at its best. The base game on its own is a rock solid design, but it feels restrictive as each faction has only one main strategy to pursue and there's no way to deviate from it if you want to stay competitive. The expansion shakes things up and offers wider variety of options. New chaos cards and upgrades give you much needed flexibility and interesting choices. Do yourself a favour and grab the expansion while you can - it's one of those must-haves. The whole package is a great, involving game.

Still, there are two things I dislike about this game:
1. Sometimes the event cards can really hurt one of the players (in a completely random and unforseeable way) and there's nothing they can do about it. 
2. There's a lot of maintenance and fiddly upkeep.

RATING: 7.5
This is one of those games. You know, the kind of game where it's not enough to simply know the rules...you have to know the strategies. And as is par for course with that kind of thing, you have to play the game multiple times before those strategies become clear to you.

See, CitOW is a game that's really intended for *exactly* 4 players and each player not only has a specific set of resources and victory condition, but more importantly, each player has a role to play. Each player kind of has another player that they need to keep in check. They need to know who that player is and how to hamper their strategy. So, in this game, you need to really get to know each player's role, what their goal is and how you fit into the equation.

I make it sound like CitOW is a complicated game but that's really not true. The rules and general game play are actually pretty simple. It's looking at the bigger picture that's the most challenging part for your first several plays.

What's my point? My point is that Chaos is not a game that I think most people will "get" the first couple of plays. Hell, I'm not even sure I fully "get it" yet. But with every play, the game shows you a little more of what's under the hood and, as a result, it gets more interesting. This game could totally climb my rankings once I've really had a consistent group going at it. Even so, I can already see why it is often so highly-regarded.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Astonished me in player interaction and spoiling plans. Probably first game which was completely fun even without victory. Beautiful, thematic. And 2009 print box art is gorgeous in quality, not visible at first sight but glossy cover on sign/artifact makes it's impression. Sadly reprint just put a plain cheap monster picture onto box.

RATING: 1
{ P - 3 [4] }

Really bothersome dilemma here in that the Khorne are insanely direct in their method of winning and essentially give the game to whomever they ignore the most if they prove unable to win. (The end to my first game was incredibly demoralizing/enlightening, as the Khorne almost exclusively win through their spinner; come last turn, if that player is unable to win, all that's left is playing king-maker.) Initial board position and spell deck luck of the draw are a huge factor; I played Tzeentch and clearly Temporal Stasis and the three 0-cost cards are all more desirable than the junky Meddling of Skaven, Dazzle, Teleport, or Persistence of Change. (Hell, Warp Shield mostly blows, the purpose of the card is simply that it cycles.) Old World cards recall the environment effects of Vegas Showdown, Game of Thrones, Pillars of the Earth, etc that randomly reward/shaft players. Like many area control games, you win not so much by strategy but rather that you happened to be attacked less.

RATING: 8
Interesting game, oozing with theme and a lot of possible strategies. Very fun asymmetrical game play.

While I like this game a lot, it does have it's failings:
Too many tokens and a map that's hard to view make working out what's going on quickly difficult. I spend a lot of time looking at the map trying to work out what the hell is going on. It has brittle playing pieces, a bad box insert, confusing rulebook, balance issues with less than 4 players and the point scoring phase of the round takes too long, involves too much maths and far, far too many tokens!

All in all a good purchase though. Tedious and irritating at times, but lots of strategy, lots of fun, would recommend.

RATING: 8
This is one of my favourite game by E. Lang. What really makes this game such a great experience, is how it makes you act and think like the character of the god you are playing. It is not only the individual victory conditions, units and special powers - it is also how you interact with your fellow gods, as it is paramount to victory that you try to hamper the progress of your "anathema" every turn ... as Khorne you hack´em to pieces, as Nurgle you let them waste away, trick them as Tzeentch or let their own desire be their doom as Slaanesh...

The only real drawback: you need the right kind of players, which can be tricky at times...

RATING: 10
Great plotting and manipulative game. Four very different gods. Lots of strategy involved. Great gameplay mechanisms. Different ways to win. Lots of replayability. Awesome map carved out of human skin. Some combinations of the 'destiny deck' can fuck up the game or better can fuck up your strategies ;).
